Greetings, Earthlings: Philip Johnston of Starcloud on Data Centers in Space

Sequoia Capital · 2026-03-17
Philip Johnston (CEO, Starcloud)
Starcloud is migrating AI compute to space to bypass terrestrial energy and land constraints, leveraging plummeting launch costs and superior solar efficiency to make space-based data centers the primary infrastructure for AI inference within the next decade.
TL;DR Starcloud is migrating AI compute to space to bypass terrestrial energy and land constraints, leveraging plummeting launch costs and superior solar efficiency to make space-based data centers the primary infrastructure for AI inference within the next decade. ...

TL;DR

Starcloud is migrating AI compute to space to bypass terrestrial energy and land constraints, leveraging plummeting launch costs and superior solar efficiency to make space-based data centers the primary infrastructure for AI inference within the next decade.

The Economic and Logistical Case for Space

  • Terrestrial Bottlenecks: Building 100MW+ energy projects on Earth currently faces 5-10 year lead times due to permitting, regulatory hurdles, and land scarcity.
  • Solar Efficiency: Space-based solar provides 8x the energy of terrestrial solar per square meter because it lacks atmospheric interference and operates in 24/7 sunlight, eliminating the need for expensive battery storage.
  • Cost Break-even: Philip Johnston estimates the breakeven point for space-based compute is a launch cost of $500/kg. As terrestrial land and power costs rise, this threshold may shift closer to $1,000/kg.
  • Marginal Cost Dynamics: Unlike Earth, where the marginal cost of each additional data center increases due to grid strain, space-based infrastructure costs decrease at scale through mass manufacturing and reusable launch vehicles.

Technical Engineering and Maintenance

  • The Heat Dissipation Problem: Because a vacuum acts as an insulator, traditional convection cooling is impossible. Starcloud uses infrared radiation and "phase change" materials to manage thermals.
      • Advanced Radiators: Next-generation satellites (Starcloud 2) will feature large deployable radiators using liquid cooling loops and heat pumps to artificially boost radiator temperature for better dissipation.
  • Radiation Resilience: To combat bit-flips and hardware degradation, Starcloud conducts extensive testing at particle accelerators (e.g., Brookhaven National Lab) to simulate five years of radiation in 24 hours.
  • Hardware Longevity: The company targets a 5-6 year useful life, mirroring the replacement cycle of terrestrial chips.
      • Reliability Data: The H100 GPU currently in orbit has reported zero restart failures, suggesting that GPU workloads (which are stochastic) are naturally resilient to minor space-induced errors.

Scaling with SpaceX Starship

  • Launch Capacity: SpaceX’s Starship is the primary catalyst. Johnston anticipates Starship will eventually launch 10MW of compute capacity per flight using a "Pez dispenser" deployment mechanism.
  • Deployment Velocity: Once Starship reaches a high flight cadence, Starcloud expects to deploy several gigawatts of new capacity monthly.
  • Inference vs. Training: Starcloud focuses on AI inference rather than frontier model training. Inference is less hindered by the speed-of-light latency (sub-50ms via Starlink) and represents the vast majority of future AI workloads.
  • Business Model: Starcloud aims to be the "Equinix of Space," providing the "box" (power, cooling, and connectivity) while allowing hyperscalers or customers to provide their own chip architectures.

Strategic Outlook and Market Predictions

  • CapEx Shift: Johnston predicts that within 10 years, close to $1 trillion in annual CapEx will flow toward space-based compute infrastructure.
  • The "Space Force" Factor: Security is managed via orbital velocity (moving at 27,000 km/h makes satellites harder to hit than stationary Earth targets) and protection from the U.S. Space Force.
  • Initial Customers: Early adoption is driven by military and government sectors needing to process high-bandwidth data (like Synthetic Aperture Radar) in-situ to bypass data downlink bottlenecks.
  • Long-term Vision: Johnston envisions a future where 99.9% of GDP is tied to inference compute, potentially moving toward a "Dyson Sphere" level of energy and compute utilization.
"Within 5 to 10 years at least half of all new compute capacity is being deployed in space for the energy... It just makes zero sense to continue building things on earth." — Philip Johnston
"The marginal cost on every additional data center you add to the [terrestrial] grid goes up... In space, the marginal cost goes down for every additional unit because you're manufacturing at rate." — Philip Johnston
"We are very quickly running up against constraints on where and how we can build new energy projects terrestrially... we're very rapidly plowing towards a brick wall." — Philip Johnston
We are thrilled to have with us today Philip Johnston, founder and CEO of Starcloud. You were the first to put data centers in space and just a few months ago, your first data center, Starcloud One, sent back the message to Earth, "Greetings earthlings, or as I prefer to think of you, a fascinating collection of blue and green." What a poetic thing to think about, AI in space looking back at us. Congratulations on what you've done. I'm excited to ask you all about data centers in space for this episode. Maybe first to get started, why build data centers in space? Yeah, so firstly thanks so much for having me, it's freaking awesome to be here. So a quick background on myself, I first, I mean, I've been interested in space my whole life. I actually spent a few years with McKinsey with the space agencies of the UA-, the different governments around the world, and that's where I started to notice that the launch cost was very rapidly coming down. And so three years ago, I kind of randomly on a weekend decided to take a trip down to Starbase Texas, where SpaceX is building the Starship launch vehicle. And I think it just blew me away the scale of the new sort of gigafactories they're building. I think they're planning to build three Starships per day or something on that order. And so the coming capacity and the potential launch cost is you know, orders of magnitude off where it is today. And so, you know, I started thinking about okay, well what is that going to enable? What new businesses will that enable? And with my co-founder Ezra, and Ezra and I go way back, we grew up in the same place in the UK. We started looking at the concept of space-based solar, which is where you have these huge solar panels in space and then you somehow beam that power down. It's not really a new idea, I mean people have been looking at this since I think Isaac Asimov in the 40s was talking about it. The problem with space-based solar is you lose most of the energy in transmission from space to Earth. And we very quickly realized, okay, well once we get that power down, you know most new energy projects on Earth today are being built to power data centers. So either directly or indirectly, that power is going to be going into data centers. And so if we can find instead a cheap way to get the data center to space, we don't lose all of that power in transmission, we can consume that power close to the source. That then became the basis of a white paper that we put out in 2024 and from there that's how that's how the company got going. Well let me ask you, so there's a distinction. I heard a lot in there on "it is possible to do data centers in space". Why do we need to do data centers in space? Maybe possible. Yeah, yeah. Why do we need to do it? Why do we need to go to space? Yeah, no it's a great question. The main reason is we are very quickly running up against constraints on where and how we can build new energy projects terrestrially to power data centers. So, for example, if you want to build a new 100 megawatt energy project, you're looking at a 5 to 10 year lead time just on the permitting, particularly in North America. And so, for example, if you want to cover 10 square kilometers of countryside with solar panels, there's a lot of people who are going to be very annoyed about that. And so you just have these, you know, we're we're very rapidly plowing towards a a brick wall where it's going to be extremely difficult to build new energy projects. We've already built in the easy places. If we could wave a magic wand and remove the regulatory constraint, what's the next constraint we run into? Well it is actually just cheaper to build things in space once the launch cost gets below a certain point. So, for example, with terrestrial solar, which is the cheapest form of energy we have, you've got three big costs. The first one I mentioned, which is the cost of permitted land. The second is the cost of battery storage and backup power. And then the last is the cost of the solar cells themselves. So in space, number one we don't need permitted land. Number two, we don't need batteries and backup power because we're 24/7 in the sun. And then lastly we need eight times less solar cuz one square meter of solar panel in space produces eight times the energy of one square meter of solar panel on Earth. And so there's a breakeven point where the launch cost, which is our main additional cost in space, where the launch cost comes below the cost of those three factors. We see that breakeven to be around 500 dollars a kilo, but as the cost of permitted land goes up, which it is, going through the roof right now, that breakeven point actually comes even closer to a thousand dollars a kilo. But but as I say, even if you even if your permitted land cost is zero, you still have those other two factors. And so at some point if you're going to build data centers anywhere, you know once the launch cost is below a few hundred bucks a kilo, you're going to do it in space because it's just cheaper. What have you learned about maintenance in space? Yeah, it's a great question as well. So for maintenance we'll be operating very similar to the way that Starlink satellites work in the in the initial years. We're not going to have robotic maintenance or anything for the first few generations at least. And so that means we need to have redundancy on the critical systems and then we overprovision things which fail over time like solar panels, you lose a few percent per year. It's very, very important that the chips do not have a higher failure rate in space than they do on Earth because the chips are one of the largest costs in this. And so a huge amount of our time, probably 70 percent of our engineering time is going onto the heat problem and the other 30 percent is going onto making the chips as reliable as possible in space. And that means a whole bunch of testing in different particle accelerators. You know we did two rounds of testing at the cyclotron proton beam accelerator in Knoxville, one round of testing at the heavy ion particle accelerator in Brookhaven National Lab, and we run it in 24 hours we can simulate five years worth of radiation, and with that then all of that data then goes into informing our choice on shielding and other software for bit-flip mitigations and things like that. But in terms of what we've learned on the first satellite, it's actually the the H100 that we have on orbit right now, we've not had a single restart failure yet or or issue that needed a restart from the chip itself. There are other areas which we we may need to put a bit more attention to, for example the power delivery and solid state drives, but the actual chip itself is extremely resilient. And GPU workloads in general are very resilient, and the reason is they're stochastic in nature. And so if you for example if you type into ChatGPT "write me a poem about space", it will give you two different poems. The the quality of the poem will be iden- will be the same, you know it'll have the exact same quality of the output, but the specific instance will be different. And so with a bit-flip on any part of that or on most of the parts of that workload, it actually doesn't make a difference to the quality of the output. So so it's actually yeah, it's it's surprisingly resilient. You said you spend the bulk of your engineering time on the heat problem. I think most people have this notion that you know space is cold and so therefore it should be an easier problem. When Sonia says most people, I I thought that was the case until a few until a few months ago. Yeah, sorry to blow your bubble. So can you just talk about, you know, what exactly is the heat dissipation problem and how what are you doing to solve it? Yeah, for sure. So yeah as you mentioned space is cold and in general that's actually once you get far enough down this rabbit hole that ends up being great. What's not great is that space is a vacuum and so obviously like a thermos flask is designed that way because a vacuum is an insulator. And so the only form of heat dissipation you can have is infrared radiation. And so everything in this world glows in infrared, if you had a camera on your face your face would be glowing in infrared. And the same is true in space, and the the amount that it glows is proportional to the temperature differential between the temperature away from your faces or away from the satellite versus it actually scales with the fourth power of the temperature. So a very small increase in the temperature increases the heat dissipation by a huge amount. And so what one of the critical things is we we need to run these radiators as hot as possible. There's a few different ways you can do that, either you can run try and run the chips as hot as possible, the problem with that is you the chips are you know have a shorter lifetime if you run them hot. The other thing you can do is there's a few ways to artificially boost the temperature of the radiator. So things like heat pipes, heat pump sorry, which you can take for example 60 degree fluid from the chips and then you can turn that into 100 degree radiator temperature with heat pumps. Got it. And so would you say the heat heat dissipation problem is like a solved problem for you all now? You obviously have one GPU in space. What's it going to take to to solve the problem for you know eventually hopefully gigawatt scale data centers in space? Yeah, how does it change as you scale? Yeah, so the first one actually has a very different thermal management system than than the next one coming up. The first one we submerged the entire motherboard, power systems, GPU and everything else in this phase change material. It's like a material that goes from solid to liquid as it as it heats up. The we can't run that continuously though, that was merely just to prove out that this works. The second one is much closer to the end state, which is it's got this enormous low cost and low mass deployable radiator. So it has liquid, we've got our custom heat sinks next to the GPUs, runs past this fluid runs past the GPUs and then out to this extremely large deployable radiator. From that one to the next one, just scaling it up, it actually is pretty simple. And so yeah we we've tested this you know in thermal and vacuum chambers and it works, we just need to now put it on orbit and make sure it actually works on orbit, and that's going to happen later this year. So it's pretty simple it's solar panels, radiators, the box like the bus, the chips and the chips obviously then come with memory, motherboard, power system although we need very small batteries. AI Is Writing the Code Now: Cisco’s Vision for the Agent Era

Six Five Media · 2026-03-17
Jeetu Patel (EVP and Chief Product Officer, Cisco)
Cisco is transitioning to an "Agent Era" where AI acts as an autonomous co-worker rather than just a tool, with plans to have 60–70% of its entire product portfolio written by AI by the end of 2027.
TL;DR Cisco is transitioning to an "Agent Era" where AI acts as an autonomous co-worker rather than just a tool, with plans to have 60–70% of its entire product portfolio written by AI by the end of 2027. This shift demands a new focus on agent-specific security and a...

TL;DR

Cisco is transitioning to an "Agent Era" where AI acts as an autonomous co-worker rather than just a tool, with plans to have 60–70% of its entire product portfolio written by AI by the end of 2027. This shift demands a new focus on agent-specific security and a heterogeneous infrastructure strategy that revitalizes the role of the CPU alongside GPUs.

The Evolution of AI-Generated Engineering

  • Product Roadmap: Jeetu Patel revealed that Cisco’s "AI Defense" is their first product 100% written by AI.
      • By the end of 2026, Cisco expects to have a half-dozen products written entirely by AI.
      • By the end of 2027, the goal is for 60% to 70% of the entire portfolio to be AI-generated.
  • Redefining Engineering Roles: Writing code currently only accounts for 30–35% of an engineer's time. AI will handle the "plumbing," allowing humans to focus on higher-level architecture, problem-solving, and judgment.
  • The New Scrum Team: Patel predicts a fundamental shift in team structure. A traditional eight-person scrum team will evolve into a team of two humans managing six AI agents.

Infrastructure: The Renaissance of the CPU and the Edge

  • Heterogeneous Compute: While the initial AI wave focused almost exclusively on GPUs, the "Agent Era" requires a mix of GPUs, accelerators, and CPUs.
  • CPU Resurgence: CPUs are seeing a "renaissance" because they are superior for the orchestration required by complex AI agents. Patel noted that the market for high-performance CPUs is currently "on fire."
  • Token Generation at the Edge: Cisco is pushing for AI processing to move out of the data center and toward the edge.
      • Unified Edge: Cisco is integrating the Nvidia Spectrum 4500 chip into its unified edge platforms to allow for simple, plug-and-play AI deployment in branch offices that lack dedicated IT support.

Security and Trust in the Agent Era

  • Agent Security as an Industry: Patel identifies agent security as a massive emerging sector. Unlike traditional security, which was the primary concern of the CISO, agent security impacts every user directly.
  • The Trust Gap: Currently, many enterprises are running agents on isolated hardware (like Mac Minis) because they do not yet trust agents on their main production networks.
  • Dynamic Guardrails: As agents become more autonomous, security must move from static rules to dynamic guardrails that can prevent an agent from making catastrophic errors, such as accidentally deleting production databases.

Key Announcements at GTC 2026

  • AI Networking: Cisco announced support for Nvidia’s Spectrum 4 and Spectrum 6 (the 100-terabit chip), providing customers with more choice in high-speed networking fabrics.
  • HyperShield & BlueField: Cisco’s HyperShield (a hybrid mesh firewall) will now support Nvidia BlueField DPUs.
  • AI Defense Integration: Cisco is positioning its AI Defense product to secure the specific agent-based technologies being released by Nvidia.

Notable Quotes

"A scrum team in the future will no longer look like eight people; it'll look like two people and six agents. And you will have four scrum teams for every scrum team you have right now." — Jeetu Patel, President and Chief Product Officer, Cisco
"You have to assume that whatever deficiencies you have in these models right now... by the time you actually get yourself prepared to take advantage of them, they will be solved. You have to fast forward three to six months." — Jeetu Patel, President and Chief Product Officer, Cisco
"Agent security is going to be a huge industry moving forward... because something going sideways with an agent would actually have meaningful consequence to the user using it." — Jeetu Patel, President and Chief Product Officer, Cisco
"There are no priors to AI. It’s too fast. It’s happening too fast and I do think there’s going to be disruption that we’re going to have to collectively make sure that you actually think through." — Jeetu Patel, President and Chief Product Officer, Cisco

Two Legendary Founders: Travis Kalanick & Michael Dell Live from Austin, Texas

All-In Podcast · 2026-03-17
Travis Kalanick (Founder and CEO, Atoms) Michael Dell (CEO, Dell Technologies)
> **Travis Kalanick:** "Truth and justice are the immune system for society.
TL;DR: Travis Kalanick officially exits stealth mode with "Atoms," a company focused on automating the physical world across food, mining, and robotics, while Michael Dell details Dell Technologies' $50B pivot toward AI infrastructure and a landmark $6.25B gift to seed...

TL;DR: Travis Kalanick officially exits stealth mode with "Atoms," a company focused on automating the physical world across food, mining, and robotics, while Michael Dell details Dell Technologies' $50B pivot toward AI infrastructure and a landmark $6.25B gift to seed investment accounts for 25 million American children.

Travis Kalanick: Exiting Stealth with "Atoms"

  • Company Rebrand: Kalanick’s holding company, City Storage Systems, has officially rebranded to Atoms. After years in stealth with thousands of employees, the company is focusing on "digitizing the physical world" by treating atoms like bits.
  • The Atoms-Based Computer: Kalanick views the physical economy through three core computing resources:
      • Manufacturing (CPU): Manipulating atoms.
      • Real Estate (Storage): Storing atoms.
      • Logistics (Network): Moving atoms from point A to point B.
  • Sector Diversification:
      • Food Infrastructure: Operates brands like CloudKitchens (US) and Kitchen Valley (Korea). The goal is to make prepared food delivery as cheap as grocery shopping.
      • Mining Automation: Atoms is in the final stages of acquiring Pronto, a San Francisco-based company automating mining equipment. Kalanick aims to make mines more productive and safety-efficient in inhospitable locations.
      • Robotics: Development of "wheelbases for robots" to provide movement for specialized, non-humanoid industrial robots.
  • Physical AI Stack: Kalanick identifies Tesla as the current leader in this space, noting that the "Physical AI stack" requires mastery of land development, chemistry, and manufacturing, not just software.

The Great Migration: Why Founders are Choosing Texas

  • California’s Decline: Kalanick and Dell discussed the "mourning process" of leaving California. Kalanick described California’s current state as "weird," citing a degradation of "truth and justice"—which he calls the "immune system for society."
  • Austin as the Future: Both founders praised Austin for its pro-growth, low-tax environment.
      • Dell’s Perspective: Texas now hosts four of the ten largest U.S. cities and produces one out of every ten children born in the U.S.
      • The "Wellspring": Michael Dell credits the University of Texas as the foundational "wellspring" for the region's tech explosion.
  • Regulatory Speed: Michael Dell highlighted that Texas "lets you build," contrasting the ease of constructing data centers and factories in Texas versus the years of bureaucratic hurdles in California.

Michael Dell: The $50B AI Infrastructure Pivot

  • Scale of AI Demand: Dell Technologies is on track for $140B in annual revenue, driven largely by a massive shift to AI infrastructure. Dell’s AI server business has grown from $2B to an estimated $50B annually.
  • The "AI Factory" Model: Dell has deployed over 4,000 "AI Factories" for enterprises. He notes that the barrier to AI adoption is rarely technology, but rather "culture, leadership, and courage" to disrupt existing business models.
  • Inference at the Edge: Dell predicts a rebalancing where intelligence moves closer to where data is created.
      • Sovereign AI: Governments and large enterprises want to keep data private and local rather than sending it to public clouds.
      • Cost Efficiency: "The lowest cost token is generated right where the data is," such as on the device or local server, to avoid the high "bill shock" of public clouds.
  • Paradigm Shift: Dell describes the transition from 60 years of "calculating computing" to a new era of "thinking machines" that accelerate scientific discovery and problem-solving.

"Invest America": A New Social Contract

  • Historic Philanthropy: Michael and Susan Dell announced a $6.25B gift to seed investment accounts for 25 million children in zip codes with median incomes under $150,000.
  • 401k from Birth: The Invest America Act creates a "401k from birth" for every American child born after January 1, 2027.
      • Account Structure: Each child receives a "Trump account" (stapled to their Social Security card) with an initial government contribution, which can then be matched by private philanthropists and parents.
      • Ownership Society: Brad Gerstner noted the goal is to reconnect the 70% of Americans who feel "left behind" by the stock market to the American Dream by making them owners of the S&P 500 from infancy.
  • The Vision: The program aims to move $5 trillion into the hands of American families over 15 years through compounding growth, bypassing traditional government "crony structures."

Notable Quotes

Travis Kalanick: "Truth and justice are the immune system for society. When the immune system is suppressed, all the social ills flare up."
Michael Dell: "The barrier to technology adoption is not technology. It's culture and leadership and courage—the willingness to change and disrupt yourself."
Brad Gerstner: "This is Social Security 2.0. It’s the biggest change to the social contract in America in 50 years... giving kids a shot to be an owner in the upside of America."
Travis Kalanick: "If you can get a meal prepared and delivered so efficiently that it starts to approach the cost of the grocery store, you do to the kitchen what Uber did to the car."
