China Global Podcast - Ep 121 ft. Da Wei

The German Marshall Fund of the United States · 2026-03-17
Da Wei (Director, Center for International Strategy and Security, Tsinghua University)
> "I think the if we are discussing the power, the hegemony, the leadership of the US, I think it's basically lies in the international institutions that US led after the World War II...
TL;DR: The upcoming March 31–April 2 summit between President Trump and Xi Jinping aims to stabilize a "stable but distracted" relationship through major trade purchases (soybeans, Boeing aircraft), though significant geopolitical breakthroughs on Taiwan or Iran are unlikely due...

TL;DR: The upcoming March 31–April 2 summit between President Trump and Xi Jinping aims to stabilize a "stable but distracted" relationship through major trade purchases (soybeans, Boeing aircraft), though significant geopolitical breakthroughs on Taiwan or Iran are unlikely due to late planning and misaligned strategic goals.

Strategic Climate and Economic Leverage

  • State of Relations: Dr. Da Wei characterizes the current relationship as stable but notes that the Trump administration is currently distracted by conflicts in the Middle East (Iran), Venezuela, and Greenland.
  • Perception of U.S. Power:
      • Leadership vs. Resources: While the U.S. remains the most powerful nation technologically and militarily, its global leadership is declining because it is "undermining the institutions it set up after WWII."
      • Strategic Assessment: Chinese scholars are debating whether U.S. military actions in Iran and Venezuela signal a reversal of U.S. decline, but no consensus exists.
  • Economic "Nuclear Weapons":
      • Mutual Assured Disruption: Both nations use specific dependencies as leverage. The U.S. utilizes chips and high-tech restrictions, while China utilizes rare earth elements.
      • Self-Defense Motive: Da Wei asserts that China’s use of leverage is defensive—intended to limit damage caused by U.S. policy—rather than offensive (e.g., China has pointedly chosen not to provide military aid to Russia or Iran).

Economic Deliverables and Trade Friction

  • U.S. Objectives: President Trump is expected to seek massive purchase agreements to reduce the trade deficit and secure mineral supply chains.
      • Commodities: Large-scale purchases of U.S. soybeans.
      • Aviation: Potential deals for up to 500 Boeing aircraft.
      • Critical Minerals: Ensuring smoother flows of rare earths, which have seen shortages affecting U.S. defense and tech sectors.
  • Beijing’s Objectives:
      • Tariff Predictability: China seeks a long-term solution to Section 301 and 232 tariffs rather than short-term renewals, which create business uncertainty.
      • Investment "White Lists": Beijing is interested in agreements that would allow Chinese companies to invest in specific U.S. sectors to create jobs and balance trade.
      • Reciprocity in Tech: If China purchases Boeing planes, it wants the U.S. to stop restricting parts for China’s domestic C919 aircraft.

Geopolitics: Taiwan and the "One China" Policy

  • Arms Sales as a Red Line: China is deeply concerned about U.S. arms sales to Taiwan.
      • Following an $11 billion package earlier this year, reports of a potential $20 billion package caused Beijing to warn that such a move could cancel future summits.
      • Trump’s decision to postpone the package is viewed by Beijing as a "smart decision" to preserve the March summit.
  • Potential for a "New Deal":
      • Transactional Diplomacy: Da Wei suggests that because President Trump is "transactional" and lacks the rigid ideological beliefs of his predecessors regarding Taiwan, there is a unique opportunity to "think outside the box."
      • Rethinking Ambiguity: Beijing wants the U.S. to move from "not supporting" Taiwan independence to actively "opposing" it, arguing that the 70-year-old policy of strategic ambiguity is increasingly dangerous.

Global Conflicts and Internal PLA Dynamics

  • The Iran Conflict:
      • Limited Cooperation: China views U.S. and Israeli actions against Iran as "unlawful" and will not take any action that appears to support the U.S. coalition (e.g., pressuring Iran or joining naval escorts in the Strait of Hormuz).
      • Summit Impact: The most optimistic outcome is a high-level joint statement calling for peace and de-escalation, intended to prevent the Iran issue from undermining the bilateral summit.
  • PLA Purges and Stability:
      • Xi’s Command: Recent investigations and removals of high-ranking Chinese military officers are described as an anti-corruption campaign that does not affect China's strategic direction.
      • Operational Control: Strategic decisions regarding the U.S. and Taiwan are made exclusively by Xi Jinping; therefore, internal military churn will not impact the summit or cross-strait stability.

***

Notable Quotes

"I think the if we are discussing the power, the hegemony, the leadership of the US, I think it's basically lies in the international institutions that US led after the World War II... From that regard, I think US is declining, because the US is destroying or kind of undermining the institution that it set up." — Dr. Da Wei
"I call it [rare earths] economic nuclear weapon. So it some people say mutual assured disruption in economic field, like the nuclear weapon as a mutual assured destruction in military field." — Dr. Da Wei
"President Trump is really hard to predict... He is a transactional president, want to do some deal. So I think that's an opportunity for the two sides to think something new [regarding Taiwan]." — Dr. Da Wei
I'm Bonnie Glaser, Managing Director of the Indo-Pacific Program at the German Marshall Fund of the United States. Welcome to the China Global Podcast. President Donald Trump is expected to visit Beijing from March 31st to April 2nd for a summit with Chinese leader Xi Jinping. It will mark the first meeting between the two leaders since they agreed to a trade truce last October that ended months of escalating tensions prompted by tariffs imposed by the United States and Chinese restrictions on rare earth exports. Planning for such a major summit usually takes place over several months, and it's getting underway quite late, causing many observers to question whether anything meaningful can be achieved. In the meantime, the United States is distracted by the ongoing conflict in the Middle East between Iran and a US-Israel coalition. To discuss the upcoming Trump-Xi summit, I'm joined today by Dr. Da Wei. He is director of the Center for International Security and Strategy and professor of international relations at Tsinghua University. Welcome to the China Global Podcast, Da Wei. It's a pleasure to have you with us in the GMF studio. "Yeah, thank you Bonnie, thank you for having me. It's a great pleasure to be joining the China Global Podcast." "Great. Well, maybe someday I will be on your podcast too." "Yeah, definitely you should, yeah. Sinopinion, right? Sinopinion. I coined this term." "It's a great name." So maybe we can just level set first. How do you view the status of US-China relations on the eve of the summit? I think since after the Busan summit, I think generally speaking, China-US relations are in a stable period. Nothing very bad happened. Of course, from China's perspective, the Taiwan arms sale package earlier this year was an issue. But except for that, I think it's generally speaking stable. At the same time, I have to say from Beijing's perspective, the Trump administration's focus is not on China right now. Obviously, there is a war in Iran and from January, we know what happened in Venezuela, then Greenland, and the controversy over Greenland. I think the priority or the focus of the Trump administration's foreign policy is not on China. It's like what you mentioned in the introduction; this administration is focusing on other issues. This, I think, casts some shadow on this upcoming summit in late March and early April. So other than perhaps distracting the United States from attention on the summit in the Indo-Pacific, do you think that the war in the Middle East has any real impact on the bilateral relationship? I think it's limited. Of course, China is watching all these developments, not only Iran, but Venezuela and other things. We are trying to understand what the foreign policy or strategy of the Trump administration is. In the National Security Strategy, it basically says that the US will focus on the Western Hemisphere. The action in Venezuela, of course, was consistent with that. But, to be honest, it's really hard to understand why the US joined the military action against Iran at this moment. It's a kind of distraction from the Western Hemisphere focus. But we are watching this. I don't think all this happened, no matter in Venezuela or Iran, fundamentally damaged China's interest. Of course, it's inconsistent and conflicts with China's position. China basically opposes these two actions, but China already expressed its opposition. I don't think it fundamentally really damaged China's interest. A lot of people in the West tend to view China's relations with Iran as a kind of axis of upheaval, or describe Iran as China's ally. But actually, Iran is not China's ally. We had good relations, but it's not a military ally. So it's not like one of our allies is being attacked. I think for China, the very important thing is that economic interests need to be protected. Of course, we expressed our opposition to that, and we hope the conflict can resolve as soon as possible. I think that's it. So I don't think it's a very big damage. Of course, it's a problem, but I don't think it's something that will make the summit cancel or postpone. I think we probably will have the summit on time as we planned. I've read that some Chinese scholars have concluded that because of the US action in Venezuela and now also its attacks on Iran, that maybe they should rethink the assessment that the United States is in decline. So I'm wondering whether you agree with that. Is that a majority view in China? I think that assessment that the East is rising and the West is declining has been shaping Chinese foreign policy and its approach to the United States. So if that assessment were changing, that would be important. Every time when the US takes military action or there is an upheaval—sometimes not military action, like if the US makes a breakthrough in technology—some people say, "You see, the US is very strong, very innovative, and militarily very powerful." There is always this kind of debate in China. I don't think there is a consensus. My personal view is that I don't think there is a majority view even on this. But I can say we have to differentiate the hegemony and the power resources. Of course, the US is a very strong country militarily, financially, and technologically. The US can make a lot of things happen. That's true. But I think if we are discussing the power, the hegemony, and the leadership of the US, I think it basically lies in the international institutions that the US led after World War II. From that regard, I think the US is declining because it is destroying or undermining the institutions that it set up. The leadership is declining. But as a country, definitely the US is still the most powerful country in the world and maybe in the future it could become even more powerful. That's possible. I won't exclude that scenario. The problem is: can the US restore the leadership? Or in China, we say this is hegemony. Can the US restore that? I think that's very hard. So if you ask me a yes or no question on whether the US is declining, I will say the US is declining gradually. But not because of the rise of China, at least not 100% because of the rise of China, but because of what the US itself chose for policy. In the run-up to summits between US and Chinese leaders in the past, US officials have often assessed the balance of leverage between the two sides. I assume that the Chinese side does the same. So at this particular juncture, how do you examine and think about the balance of leverage? What are China's sources of leverage? What are US sources of leverage? I think this is a very hard question. I think the US has a lot of leverage against China. China has some. China is a nuclear country, and the US is nuclear too, so nuclear weapons are something we can use to deter each other. And last year we had the rare earth controversy. Many people believe, and I agree, that China uses rare earth as leverage. I sometimes call it an economic nuclear weapon. Some people say it is mutual assured disruption in the economic field, like nuclear weapons are mutual assured destruction in the military field. In the supply chain, the US uses other leverage against China, like chips and other high-tech restrictions. We are using those leverages against each other. But the logic here is like nuclear weapons. The US has more nuclear weapons than China and more advanced nuclear weapons than China. But once you have credible deterrence to the other side, it works. China does not need 2,000 nuclear heads. That's too expensive. Maybe in the future, if China has 1,000, that's enough. On the economic leverage, the US has more leverage against China. That's true. But when you have things like rare earth, or some leverage in the pharmaceutical side—which I hope China will never use—it is true that we still have at least several leverages at hand. So we don't need to have equal numbers of leverage against each other. So long as we can deter each other, I think then we will have okay relations on the economic side. Economically and diplomatically, I don't think China and the US are using these leverages against each other because our goals are different. The US is trying to leverage China to force China to—I don't know—what is the US goal? This is a question maybe for you. Some people in China believe the US tries to slow China's development. Some people say the US wants to limit China's influence or shape China's future. But from China's side, our goal in using leverage is to stop what the US side is doing. We try to limit the damage that the US side is causing to us. From China's perspective, using those leverages is a kind of self-defense. Of course, the US side also says they are self-defending. But again, if you are self-defending, I don't think we really need so much leverage to make the US situation difficult. In the Middle East, some people imagine that China can support Iran or Russia militarily by giving them weapons. That would make the US or the West in bigger trouble. China has never done that. I don't think China wants to do that. So we are not using those potential leverages against the US or the West. This is not China's goal. Let's focus on the summit. I think for the United States, at least my expectation is that Trump wants to ensure that China will purchase large quantities of US soybeans. There are reports of maybe up to 500 Boeing aircraft. In addition, I think he wants to ensure smoother flows of rare earths and critical minerals, which according to media reports continue to be problematic. There have been shortages and this is affecting many US businesses. Of course, also US defense companies, but China has said that it will not sell rare earths to US companies if they will end up being used in the defense sector. So what do you think Beijing wants to achieve at this summit? Is the focus on tariffs, economic trade issues, or is there an interest in talking about geopolitical matters? I think Beijing definitely first wants this summit to happen and go smoothly. This is a signal or gesture to the world that the two sides are talking to each other and the two presidents have good relations. This is the signal China wants to send to the world, to China, and to the US. In the US there are a lot of different views about US-China relations, so we want to use this image to stabilize the longer-term relationship. This is the most important thing. Then more specifically, economically for China, I will say tariffs definitely are in the top position on the agenda. I'm not so sure after the Supreme Court decision here in the United States about Trump's tariffs to China. From China's perspective, that's not a bad thing because it lowered the tariff on China, but it makes things more complicated. The Trump administration may be thinking of the new 301 tariffs and 232 tariffs after the current 122 section 15% tariff expires. That will expire in July, which is three months after the summit. Originally, I hoped last year that the two sides could solve the problem in April by announcing something long-term. But now I think it's more complicated because if you announce something, the Trump administration may change its policy again in July or August. But at least from our perspective, tariffs are an important thing. We want stability and predictability. It's not just about the level of the tariff, but the predictability. We don't want to just renew it or postpone it for another year or several months. That creates uncertainty for the business environment. From the Chinese side, investment is another thing a lot of people are discussing, particularly Chinese investment in the United States. That depends on the Trump administration's decision, for instance, to create a so-called "white list" that allows Chinese companies to invest in certain areas. If we can reach an agreement over that, I think it's good for the bilateral relations and for balancing the trade relations. It's also good for creating jobs for the United States. So I think that's another thing a lot of people in Beijing are discussing. Purchases are a relatively easy thing. China, for every reason, needs Boeing airplanes. I don't know if it's 500, but we have already purchased a lot. Actually, I took a Boeing airplane to the United States this time. And of course, China also wants the US to act; like we purchase Boeing airplanes, the US needs to make the supply of parts for our C919 big airplane smooth. The US slowed, if not stopped, the supply of parts, making their production plan very slow. So the Chinese side wants the US to do something about these restrictions and export control measures. These are on our list for this summit. But you mentioned geopolitical issues; to be honest, I don't think we have enough time. I could be wrong, but now we only have less than 20 days. I don't know whether the two sides, except for the economic and trade teams, really have serious and in-depth communication over the deliverables for the summit. I have not heard of that. Maybe I don't have the information, but normally when the US president goes to China, the Secretary of State goes weeks before to prepare. Wang Yi and Marco Rubio met in Europe in February, but I don't know if they prepared the deliverables for that. So I'm quite cautious about geopolitical outcomes. If we can announce something on economic and trade, we have another two or three meetings this year. We can leave something for the later summits. Well, you didn't mention Taiwan, and there's been a lot of speculation that Beijing wants new language on Taiwan. Some people have indicated it may be a very high priority. So, why is new language necessary and what specific language do you think that Beijing wants to hear from President Trump? You mean for this April summit? I'm a little bit suspicious that we will have a major breakthrough on Taiwan. We already discussed geopolitical issues, and Taiwan is the most sensitive and difficult issue on the plate. But Taiwan is an issue, and for this summit, I think maybe the arms sale issue is more important. What specifically do you think that Xi Jinping wants from President Trump on arms sales to Taiwan? No arms sales in the short run. Of course, President Xi will say you should never do this; this is China's policy. But realistically, if the US sells another big package this year, that will damage our upcoming visits in the fall and winter. The US has already had a more than 11 billion dollar arms sale earlier this year, which I think is the largest package. After that, US media reports that the US is considering another 20 billion. I don't know if that's true or not, but in March that's something the Chinese side really worries about. Some people in China link that to the prospect of the March-April summit: if there is another package, no summit. I guess that was one of the topics in President Xi and President Trump's phone call in early March. I guess they must have spent some time on that. Then, President Trump said the US postponed this package. I think that's a smart decision. For this upcoming summit, I think President Xi will emphasize this issue again and try to help President Trump understand the importance and sensitivity of it. But I'm still quite cautious that the Chinese side will push the US side for new rhetoric or new policies on Taiwan. Not because we don't want it, but because I don't think we have enough time. But again, I could be wrong because President Trump is really hard to predict. Maybe he may say something. But for me, this is something we need to consider in the later meetings this year or next year. The Chinese side definitely wants the US side to say something or do something. From China's perspective, the Taiwan question needs to be solved. We don't have a timetable, but the Chinese side definitely believes that the country must be reunified. I'm not saying this is just a theoretical prospect; the Chinese side is working towards that goal. If the US side continues its current position, which I think has kept a very ambiguous position on Taiwan's sovereign status, that conflicts with China's goal. If this ambiguity continues, the situation over the Taiwan Strait could become more and more dangerous given this gap between the Chinese and American positions. If China and the US want to avoid military conflict in the future, we need to narrow this gap. That's the reason we try to push President Trump to say something. I think we think that President Trump is different from the Republican and Democratic presidents we have worked with before. He does not have very strong beliefs or a fixed position on the Taiwan issue. I don't think he views Taiwan in the traditional view that his predecessors had. He is a transactional president who wants to do a deal. So I think that's an opportunity for the two sides to think of something new. The US has kept this ambiguous position since at least the 1970s, since Nixon. But I can also say it's since 1950 after the Korean War. It's already been almost more than 70 years. Many things have changed in the world. I think the US side should work with the Chinese side to think of a position that can prevent our two countries from going to war. I want to follow up briefly. Some people have speculated that Xi Jinping would like President Trump to say that the United States "opposes" Taiwan independence instead of "does not support" independence, or even go further and endorse peaceful unification. But neither one of those would necessarily mean a US declaration that Taiwan is not a sovereign entity. So what you're saying is actually going further than a rhetorical change. I think I went further, it's true. But I want to go to the essence of what China is thinking or asking for. By pushing the US side to say "we oppose Taiwan's independence," that is actually inconsistent with the US One China policy as currently stated. That would mean the US at least excludes one prospect for Taiwan, which is independence. Currently, I don't think the US excludes any prospect, whether it's independence, reunification, or maintaining the status quo. By saying they oppose independence, that already goes beyond the One China policy; it means a change in policy from the US side. The essence is that the US needs to rethink its One China policy. I don't know if that's doable, particularly even if let's imagine President Trump says something. Maybe the next president or the Congress could balance it with something even worse. So, I don't know if it's doable, but I just try to explain the thinking behind the demand for "opposing Taiwan's independence." For me actually it's good, but it's already out of the box. The box is the One China policy. You've already gone out of the box. It's a correct direction for me, but even that is not good enough from my perspective. But even this first step, I think, is extremely difficult. I hope the two sides can be more creative, but in practice, I'm not very optimistic. Earlier you mentioned investment as a possible issue that the two presidents could discuss. There have been some reports that there might be a Chinese investment fund, and President Trump himself has said that he is interested in increasing Chinese investment in the United States. US companies are also interested in expanding market access in China. Do you think there could be progress on these issues at this particular summit? I have not heard any in-depth discussion on that issue recently. I heard a lot of discussion like three months ago, but I have not heard any preparation for that. So I don't know; it's really hard. Nowadays it's really hard for researchers to anticipate the outcome deliverables for this summit. Sometimes just two presidents make the decision, or the leaders of the economic teams, like Vice Premier He Lifeng and Secretary Mnuchin, spend one day and suddenly announce something big. For us outsiders, what I can say is I have not heard it in recent weeks. So that makes me feel it's not very close to happening, but who knows? Maybe the two leaders will announce it. That's possible. But I will say the chance is not very high. I think that Iran will almost certainly be discussed and I wonder if you think there's any space for US-China cooperation on Iran. There is a possibility Beijing could put some pressure or encourage Iran to take certain actions if Trump is interested in winding down this war. If the Straits of Hormuz remain closed, China could offer to send some navy ships to help escort commercial ships and to keep the strait open, either with the United States or in parallel. Do you see any space for cooperation on Iran? My instinct is that China will not do anything viewed as being against Iran. We think the US and Israel violated international law by attacking another sovereign state. Of course, the reason is complicated because of the nuclear issue, but this military action from China's perspective is unlawful. So I don't think China will do something viewed as against Iran or helping the US and Israel. It's not fair from our perspective. So things like pressing the Iran side or sending the navy to the Hormuz Strait—even though it's not directly against Iran—I think it could be viewed by the Iranian side as being along with the US and Israel. I don't think China will do that. But I'm not saying there is completely no opportunity for cooperation. Though I don't think the likelihood is very high, I hope the two leaders can work together. President Trump always says he wants to be a president of peace. I hope the two leaders can jointly work on de-escalating the conflict or even announce something for peace in Iran. I think that could be viewed very positively by all the countries in the world. The two leaders can even announce something in principle. I don't think they need to do something very specific. Like the two leaders can say, "we want to see the war end as soon as possible." I think that's good enough. I really think for China and the US to work on Iran specifically is too difficult. Our positions and interests are very different. So my bottom line is we should not let the Iran issue undermine this summit. If we can prevent that, I think I'm satisfied. They can say something maybe not very specific, but beautiful. I think that as each day passes, everybody who is observing the US-China relationship in the run-up to the summit is lowering expectations for what could be produced. So we still have a few weeks; we'll see how low the expectations go. The last question I wanted to ask is about the purges of military officers in China. Here in the United States, people have been paying a lot of attention to that. My question is whether you think that this will have any implications for the US-China relationship. Certainly, it occurs to me that we really haven't seen very much in the way of US-China military exchanges in recent months. Some people think that there could be even some implications for cross-strait relations. I was recently in Taiwan and people were remarking how there had been very little PLA Air Force activity against Taiwan. In fact, I think there was a period of seven straight days where there were absolutely no PLA Air Force operations across the center line. Does this have any implications for the summit? I think sometimes the outside world overinterprets many things in China, like the case you mentioned regarding China's navy and air force activity near Taiwan. When you have a lot of activity, they have a lot of speculation. When you don't have it, there is another set of speculation. I don't have an explanation for that, but for this anti-corruption campaign in China's military, I don't think that will have any impact on China-US relations. And I think that will have very little impact on the Taiwan issue. I think the PLA is under the absolute control of the party and of President Xi. I don't think other people who have been investigated or detained for corruption reasons have really strong influence on very important issues like China-US relations and the Taiwan question. These are the two issues I think are definitely decided by President Xi. I don't think anybody under him, no matter in the Central Military Commission or in other places, has such an important influence over the decision. So yeah, that may have some impact at the operational level. That's true. But at the strategic level or who makes the decision, I think that's President Xi, very clearly. In the short run, when an official is investigated, you don't have someone to talk to, but very soon we will have replacements. The two militaries can continue to talk and work together. So I don't worry about that very much. Maybe after a few months, we may see new people. Particularly after the summit, the two presidents could instruct the two militaries to talk to each other. So I don't worry about that very much. We've been talking with Dr. Da Wei, who is director of the Center for International Security and Strategy and professor of international relations at Tsinghua University. Thanks so much for joining us. "Thank you Bonnie, thank you for having me."

The Investor Behind a16z’s America First Push On Dealing With the Pentagon ‘Where Bits Meet Atoms’

Semafor · 2026-03-17
David Ulevitch (General Partner, Andreessen Horowitz)
David Ulevitch, head of Andreessen Horowitz’s $1.776 billion "American Dynamism" fund, details a strategic shift in Silicon Valley toward "heavy" industries—defense, energy, and manufacturing—where advanced software can disrupt legacy government contractors.
TL;DR David Ulevitch, head of Andreessen Horowitz’s $1.776 billion "American Dynamism" fund, details a strategic shift in Silicon Valley toward "heavy" industries—defense, energy, and manufacturing—where advanced software can disrupt legacy government contractors. The core...

TL;DR

David Ulevitch, head of Andreessen Horowitz’s $1.776 billion "American Dynamism" fund, details a strategic shift in Silicon Valley toward "heavy" industries—defense, energy, and manufacturing—where advanced software can disrupt legacy government contractors. The core thesis posits that private venture capital can solve national security and infrastructure challenges more efficiently than traditional government-funded R&D by applying Silicon Valley’s high-speed, high-automation "bits meet atoms" approach.

The "American Dynamism" Investment Thesis

  • Target Sectors: The fund focuses on categories serving the national interest, including defense, aerospace, energy resilience, supply chain, public safety, and domestic manufacturing.
  • "Bits Meet Atoms": Ulevitch defines the strategy as investing in the intersection of advanced software and physical hardware.
      • Technological Shift: The goal is to replace expensive, "exquisite" hardware with commodity hardware driven by sophisticated software (e.g., using phone optics and software to replace $25,000 vacuum-tube night vision systems).
  • Market Durability: While "capital-light" SaaS models are facing devaluation due to AI, Ulevitch argues that hardware-heavy businesses with high automation and verticalized supply chains offer high-margin, unconstrained upside.

Disrupting the Defense Industrial Base

  • Critique of Legacy Primes: Ulevitch argues that major incumbents (the "Primes") are disincentivized to move fast or lower costs because their profit margins are often baked into "cost-plus" contracts where going over budget increases total profit.
  • Economic Asymmetry: Current military engagements highlight a cost crisis; the U.S. is using multi-million dollar Patriot missiles to intercept $250,000 Iranian-style drones.
      • Startup Solution: Startups like Anduril focus on high-rate, low-cost production using the "Elon philosophy" where the factory itself is the primary product.
  • Procurement Shift: Ulevitch advocates for a model where the government stops funding endless R&D and instead buys finished commercial products that meet specific capability specs, shifting the R&D risk to venture capitalists.

AI, Ethics, and the "God Complex"

  • The OpenAI/Anthropic Conflict: Addressing the controversy regarding AI companies working with the Pentagon, Ulevitch views the choice as a "sorting algorithm" for talent.
  • Opposition to "Extrajudicial" Decisions: Ulevitch criticizes CEOs who refuse to sell to the government based on personal moral objections, calling it a "god complex."
      • The Argument: Private citizens lack the classified intelligence (satellite imagery, wiretaps) necessary to make better tactical decisions than the chain of command.
      • Smart Weapons: He argues AI will make weapons "smarter," allowing them to distinguish between a school bus and a tank more accurately than humans, thereby reducing collateral damage.

Energy Abundance and Infrastructure

  • Nuclear Renaissance: The fund is heavily invested in nuclear energy (e.g., Radiant), viewing it as the cleanest and safest technology for meeting the "insatiable thirst" for power driven by AI data centers and EVs.
  • Regulatory Acceleration: AI is being used to drastically reduce the time needed to process complex regulatory and transport permits for nuclear facilities.
  • The Venn Diagram of Security: Ulevitch argues that energy, space, and defense are now inseparable; without domestic energy abundance and space-based communications, national security is impossible.

Scaling Venture Capital to Trillion-Dollar Outcomes

  • Outcome Potential: Ulevitch pushes back against critics who say VC doesn't scale to heavy industry, citing SpaceX as a potential trillion-dollar outcome.
  • The Capital Stack: While VCs take the early equity risk, these companies eventually utilize private credit and real estate financing to build massive factories and data centers.
  • Public Safety and Surveillance: Defending investments like Flock Safety (license plate readers), Ulevitch argues that the benefit of solving crimes outweighs privacy concerns, noting that most privacy violations occur via data brokers and cell phones rather than transparent public safety tools.
"The American Dynamism fund is a fund... focused on investing in companies that we say serve the national interest... it is where atoms meet bits, which historically has not been in vogue for the venture capital industry." — David Ulevitch
"I actually think [the decision to work with government] is a sorting algorithm... there’s other people that say, 'we elect leaders, the leaders make laws, there’s lots of oversight... and I trust that democratic system.'" — David Ulevitch
"If I had to bet on which end of the barbell will get to very high rates of production more quickly, is it the legacy incumbent primes who lack the institutional knowledge or appetite to move very, very fast, or the startups who have just survived an entire period of life of not dying? I would make that bet every single day of the week." — David Ulevitch
Welcome to Compound Interest from Semafor Business. I'm Rohan Goswami, Semafor's business reporter, and I'm joined, as always, by Liz Hoffman, our business and finance editor. Where are you joining us from today, Liz? Hi Rohan, I'm in Semafor's offices in downtown Washington, D.C. down here this week, and it's a great backdrop for the conversation we're going to have today. We're talking to David Ulevitch, who's a partner at Andreessen Horowitz, also known as a16z, the big venture capital firm. He runs their American Dynamism fund, which is kind of the "America First" fund inside kind of the OG "America First" firm. You know, a16z was kind of ground zero for this ideological shift in Silicon Valley. Their founder, Marc Andreessen, was an early convert to Donald Trump's second-term campaign and agenda, and has sort of epitomized this investing shift towards national security, defense, space, robotics, energy, and minerals. Real things. Real things, but they all kind of have this slightly nationalist, slightly hawkish, and slightly kind of right-coded undertone. It is also interesting culturally at a16z. It is a remarkable shift for a firm where Marc Andreessen's "Software Is Eating the World" essay was iconic. Now you're watching them sort of realign and try and find a new blueprint in a world which has been totally scrambled and upended by AI. Yeah, and that's why I really wanted to have David on the show. The entire investing skillset and ecosystem of Silicon Valley was sort of oriented around capital-light, infinitely scalable code. They sort of very proudly built light stuff. You know, we got an Uber and then we got an Uber for everything else. They were backing these business models that took very little physical risk, basically never touched hardware, almost never touched people, and were kind of marketplaces connecting tailors with people who needed their pants hemmed and kind of taking a vig there. But yeah, they very proudly built things that didn't weigh very much. That's sort of the simplest way to see the transformation in venture; they're building really heavy, really expensive stuff. And the question is, why? That is always the question. How much of that is that they just like this stuff? Just sort of ideologically, they have shifted towards a more conservative, more America-First thing, and so they want to put their money there, regardless of whether they have a real right to win. And then B, are they going to be any good at it? Huge parts of the way the U.S., and this is happening to some degree globally, builds national security is now in the hands of people who haven't done it before. David is absolutely the right person to talk to about all this, so let's take a quick break and we'll come right back with him. Every business starts with an idea, but how can you go from daydreamer to industry leader? Amazon Business accelerates your journey with smart business buying capabilities. You can get everything you need to grow in one familiar place, from office supplies to IT essentials and maintenance tools. Amazon Business takes the buying experience you know and love from Amazon plus tools that help you save costs and make insights-based decisions. Ready to bring your vision to life? Learn how at amazonbusiness.com. David, welcome to the show. Thank you for having me. Good to be here. So just to start, what is a16z's American Dynamism fund and what is the origin story there? Okay, you want the long answer or the short answer? We want the long answer. If it's too long, we'll cut it down. Always, we want the long answer always. Okay, the long answer. The American Dynamism fund is a fund that we have here at Andreessen Horowitz that is focused on investing in companies that we say serve the national interest. That's a broad term which largely means investing in defense, aerospace, space broadly, energy, the supply chain, public safety, and manufacturing. These are all things that contribute to the foundations of how we live and work that promote national security, that promote economic security and resilience, and sort of make this the place that we all are excited to either be from, come to, build our businesses in, live in, and make the American dream more real. On top of that, it is a broader sort of umbrella that encompasses lots of things around education and healthcare, things that we may not invest in but we will often talk about because they're important. The other thing that I think is distinctive for American Dynamism is not just the focus on the categories of defense and energy, public safety, etc., but often these are companies that sit at the intersection of very, very advanced software as well as hardware. It's where atoms meet bits, which historically has not been, I would say, in vogue for the venture capital industry, certainly over the last 25 years, although now it's becoming more popular, I think probably due to us. So that's largely what American Dynamism is, and then I'm happy to tell you about the origin story about how it came about, both as a firm practice as well as for myself. I ran a cybersecurity company called OpenDNS for a long time. I was never a veteran, I never served our country, and I didn't even know that many veterans, but then my company got bought by Cisco. Two things happened. One was that I got to work with a lot of veterans. Cisco hires a lot of people that served our country as they come out of the service. And then the other thing that happened at Cisco is I became an executive sponsor for different customers that were parts of federal government. It was kind of like a glorified escalation point inside the company for people that were part of the intelligence community or people that were part of the Pentagon. If they would go out and buy 50 firewalls and would be told that it might take four months or six months for delivery and they didn't like that, they would call me and escalate. But I ended up getting to spend a lot of time with those customers and really grew to realize and learn that the people that worked for our country in the armed forces or just in the federal government or in the intelligence community were just super apolitical. They were very patriotic, they deserved the best technology and capabilities, and they often did not have them. They just wanted to make sure that the American experiment would continue and that we would remain the world superpower, and they were just very, very patriotic in their mission. So when I left Cisco, I had met Marc and Ben. Ben made the mistake of saying if I joined Andreessen Horowitz, I could do whatever I wanted. In about a year after I joined, I really was trying to think about how I wanted to spend my time. My background was in enterprise software, but I was much more excited about doing something that I thought would have very long-term meaningful impact. Marc introduced me to Palmer Luckey, the founder of Anduril. I co-led the Series B of Anduril with a woman named Katherine Boyle, who was at another firm at the time. That's how we met, so we were rivals. I think she probably didn't even like me because she thought she was going to do the deal herself. But then fast forward, she and I got to know each other better and we talked about deals. About two years later toward the end of 2021, I ended up recruiting her. I said, "Hey look, I believe in this opportunity that we have to invest in introducing these fundamental technology shifts into these categories like defense and public safety and space and energy. Let's make a full practice out of this." She was putting to paper a lot of the things I believed in, so I hired her at the end of 2021 and we launched the American Dynamism practice in 2022. Then we raised a fund and now we're on our second fund and off to the races. I'm curious, American Dynamism is pretty neutral, and all the things you're talking about, national security and energy, in theory should be bipartisan or apolitical. Maybe I'm just in Washington this week and so maybe I'm just simmering in the partisan stew. But what do you make of the fact that a lot of these areas feel right-coded these days? Is that a problem for you as you think about fundraising or investing? Yeah, actually I think one of the things that I've discovered—our firm is very active in politics and policy, so I get to see this stuff firsthand—is that most of these issues are actually very bipartisan. They may not always feel bipartisan in the talking points, but when you really spend time one-on-one with policymakers, they're very bipartisan. I think they feel right-coded because we have an administration that loves to lean into the partisanship and the rhetoric. And frankly, we have Democrats on the left who love to lean into their view of that same conversation, the other side of the coin. But when it comes to getting work done, there's a reason that a lot of these critical committees are all by definition bipartisan. What's right-coded to me feels like the momentum and desire for change and the appetite to probably break a little bit of glass by making change. That probably does feel maybe more right-coded because we have an administration that again leans very heavily into the idea that we don't care about the way they used to do it, and we don't care about what was done in the past, and we have to do things differently. I actually think that hopefully will continue no matter what the next administration leadership looks like, because I think that people recognize that we have a system of incumbents who are very slow-moving and stodgy and not using the newest and most advanced technology. We have production and manufacturing capabilities that have really slowed down and atrophied and been outsourced. We have put too much supply chain resilience on people that may not be able to supply us in the future or may even be adversaries. So I think that the right-coded piece is really just the appetite for rapid radical change, but not... I think the core issues—energy abundance, we have an insatiable thirst for energy—don't matter if it's for AI data centers or for electric vehicles or for national security. We just have an insatiable thirst for energy. That's a fairly bipartisan view. You wrote last August, "We believe investors, not government, hold the key to defending America's future." That is a pretty bold statement. What did you mean by that? It's not appropriate for the government to endlessly fund advanced R&D that may not have an ROI. We will take on that risk as venture capitalists where we think we can find the best entrepreneurs, and we think we can motivate the best founders to apply the best technology to manufacture things and then just sell them to the government. I think people don't always realize that that's not the traditional way that the government has been doing things like defense R&D broadly for the last 70 years. What normally happens is the government will say we need this capability and we will give hundreds of millions of dollars to a major prime contractor to work on this for a while. Then the contractor will take much longer to do it and it will go way over budget, but the profit margin's already baked in for that prime contractor, the incumbent. It doesn't matter that it's over budget or that it took too long because, you know, if something was supposed to cost 100 million but then it cost 200 million but your profit margin's guaranteed to be 10%, well, you actually just doubled your profit margin by going over budget. This is true and look, for things like aircraft carriers, maybe that's appropriate because it's a very exquisite platform. But when it comes to things where we want things that are low cost, high volume, and a high rate of production like drones, there actually is a better way. A commercial way is better where we fund the private capital and then the government will just buy it if it meets their capability specs. Let's talk about drones actually because I did see some crazy stat about basically how asymmetric the weapons that we're using against—in Iran specifically—the Shahed drones are. We're using these hundred-million-dollar weapons platforms that are in very limited quantities against these really cheap lightweight drones that the Iranians are fielding. You've talked about the process and how the process from your eyes and a lot of people's eyes is broken. What should we actually be building? Like what are we not building in the US that you want to invest in more of, or that you are invested in and think is really critical? Yeah, well I mean I think advanced interception capability that's at a high rate of production and at a low cost is a perfect example. We have phenomenal weapons capabilities and we have things like the Patriot missile, but you cannot take a Patriot missile which could cost anywhere from 5 to 8 million dollars per shot and use it against something that costs a quarter of a million dollars. Not only do we have limited quantity, but the asymmetric economic cost is incredible to take something down that costs a quarter of a million dollars and of which there's essentially an unlimited supply of the Shahed drone. They keep making more of them. Russia now makes essentially an equivalent Shahed drone and so you need to have something that's both economically viable and where you're not losing the economic asymmetry. You also need to be able to do high rates of production. That's something that the primes have not demonstrated any ability to do or appetite to do despite multiple requests from the government to speed up production and to open up new factories. I think the demand signal from the government is now very, very clear, but there are startups that say, "Hey wait a minute, we understand the Elon philosophy of the factory is the product." We actually can focus on advanced automation and applying robotics to these processes to verticalizing more of our supply chain and manufacturing capabilities. We can actually switch out some of the components for lower-cost components, and we can use software for the guidance systems and the command and control systems and not have to use very, very exquisite laser systems or optical systems. And so that's happening now. We're in the very early innings of seeing this on the battlefield. In Iran, we're seeing now that low-cost drones are being used. I think this is probably the last major military engagement where we will not see these new startups have a much, much larger position on the battlefield than they have today. There's pockets of it in today's battlefield, but I think this is the last fight where it'll be largely incumbent driven. The other big storyline in defense over the last couple weeks is Anthropic. Let's talk about the Anthropic of it all; the company is in the middle of a huge fight with the Pentagon. OpenAI came in and took the contract, and Sam Altman seems to have some regrets about how that all worked out too. Did the calculus of doing business with the US military just change? I think that from my vantage point as a venture capitalist—I'm in New York but ostensibly in Silicon Valley... They let you keep your VC card when you moved to New York City? They do. Yeah, it's nice of them. I actually think a lot of this has to do with talent and it's almost like it's a sorting algorithm. If you think back to like when Google employees protested Project Maven, that became a sorting algorithm for Google where a lot of people said, "Hey, I want to work at a place that refuses to make sure that our men and women who serve this country have the best capabilities." Like, they don't want to work at that company or they do want to work at that company, and it kind of sorts people out. And I think with the Anthropic and OpenAI decisions to support or not support working with the government and letting the government decide, it's kind of that same sorting algorithm. There's going to be employees who leave OpenAI and say that they don't want to be a part of OpenAI for whatever reason. They don't agree with OpenAI's decision to work with the government, or they believe that there should be an extrajudicial process and CEOs of companies should be able to overrule the government and they want to go work at Anthropic. They kind of like the god complex CEO mentality and they want to be part of that. I think there's other people that say, "Well, we elect leaders, the leaders make laws, there's lots of oversight, there's ways to provide judicial oversight and congressional oversight, and I trust that democratic system." And so I also want the people that serve our country to have the best capabilities, and those people will stay at OpenAI. It's interesting because, you know, with Anthropic, there was some self-sorting that happened on the way into the founding of these companies. But it's one thing for the Pentagon to say, "Look, we don't like Anthropic, we are mad at Dario and we're canceling the contract." That is a thing that happens all the time and should in military contracting. It's another to like try to put a private company out of business, which, depending on who you ask, is sort of what would happen if no company that did any kind of business with the Pentagon was allowed to use Anthropic in any of its systems. That seems both highly unlikely and also not exactly what's happening. I think that people that have watched this administration know that they sometimes say the extreme form of something and then they generally do something that is not nearly as extreme as what their original headline rhetoric is. I think Anthropic is doing incredibly well. I think it's a very impressive company, and I think it's their decision not to work with the government. I think that decision will have consequences, like any decision you make in business. I don't think it will be a company-killing event; I think the company is a generational company. I think there's plenty of other companies that say, "Wait a minute, not only do we want to work with the government, we're fine with it, but we don't want to put ourselves in the position of trying to make extrajudicial decisions." They don't want to decide how technology is used by these customers who have far more information and intelligence and insight into the use cases and the situation. I can't even personally imagine ever putting myself in that situation. Not just because of the legal liability it would open me up to, but just like the moral liability of why... I don't want to be the person they call and say, "Can we hit the enter button on this command or not?" What a crazy dynamic to want to be in. But I also don't have a god complex. Naturally, I would not want to be in that position. But if I did have a god complex then I'd be like, "Yes, you can just call me every time you want to use my software and ask me if it's okay to use it." You had Emil Michael on stage I think last week or two weeks ago. This is the deputy undersecretary in charge of a lot of the Pentagon's tech use. I think he made a compelling case. Dario's view, which is one that I think is understandable, is that the technology has moved... it's always been the case that technology is often far ahead of legislation. That's generally always a true statement. And I think Dario's view is that this technology is so advanced, so far beyond other capabilities that have been ahead of legislation, that we actually can't just rely on legislation catching up because the window of time between when legislation catches up and policy catches up is too wide and the potential consequences are too great. That's the argument that I would probably make. The challenge with that is that there has to be another solution. One solution could just be like, I'm not going to sell to the government, which is currently where Anthropic is. But you have to recognize that when the folks who are making the decisions of how to use this technology, they have all of the intelligence information coming in from the clandestine services. They have all the intelligence information coming from the Department of War, from the special operations groups, from the special missions units. They're the ones who have all the foreign information around wiretaps and data of like where is Maduro hiding out, what building is he in. They're using all this information to synthesize a plan that will protect the most American lives and achieve their mission outcomes. Not only is it illegal, but it's impossible to convey all that information to some third-party extrajudicial person who's not part of the chain of command. That also doesn't make any sense either. And so there has to be some other path that I think needs to emerge and one can be that the technology might be more limited in its capabilities, or there will be some companies that will fine-tune their models to make better decisions in those use cases. We're not talking about do we or do we not have weapons, what we're really talking about is do we have incredibly smart weapons. This is a business show, so we want to sort of talk about the business of VC and how it is changing. VC grew up investing in software, at least over the last 25 or 30 years, sort of very proudly kind of capital-light and infinitely scalable. Why do you have a right to win in these hardware-heavy, capital-intensive businesses? I will answer your question but I think there's a meta-answer first, which is that things that were previously held to be sort of unassailable truths are now coming into question. People thought SaaS was like the most durable business model of all time and if you invested in an enterprise SaaS company, it was incredible forever; it would pay out like an annuity. Then AI came along and said well wait a minute, the ability to replace your internal IT tools with something that's perfectly built the way you want for your own internal system now is like infinitely easier and you don't need to pay ServiceNow anymore. These companies that were perceived to be unassailable now it's like well these things aren't even worth really anything. Maybe they're worth four times earnings or five times earnings, I don't know, but it's no longer these 50 times earnings because they're no longer unassailable. And then previous conceived beliefs around hardware being so hard or low margin being impossible to work... well, that's not true when we start building automation into the factory or taking control of our supply chain. We start using advanced software to replace things that had to be previously done in software. We actually think that with a high rate of production, you can get high-margin outcomes. And look, there's tailwinds in defense and energy and aerospace right now. If you look at everything from what SpaceX has done to unlock launch capability, to what's happening in cyberspace as well as electronic warfare, the opportunity set just is growing. We think that again, the opportunity set is growing largely without bound and in a way that I think will be much higher margin than in the past. So I can't promise that but that's what I believe; that's what we're betting and that's what we're investing against. And we're taking these categories where historically founders have worked for a long time—no offense to them—but we're now bringing the best Silicon Valley mindset. We are bringing the best founders into these largely legacy slow-moving categories and actually applying advanced technology to them in a way that I think will be much, much more compelling and deliver much greater returns. So I think that's my answer as to why now. We have this watershed technology moment and the economic demand side is just growing without bound. You say that something about the way that the hardware is being produced has some DNA that is borrowed from or grafted from other things that venture has done in the past. One sort of just marginal example is that night vision typically has had to use vacuum tubes. I remember reading about this. This is still true today. When you go buy these very expensive exquisite night vision systems, you know, it's like 10,000 or 25,000 dollars and it literally has vacuum tubes in it. I'm not an expert in this but I'm pretty sure I'm correct about that. But new night vision systems—because if you think about the cameras we have with all the multiple lenses going on—can do a long exposure to capture all the light. You can do a quick exposure to get the granularity, and then software basically now can provide night vision capabilities almost as good or better without any night vision hardware. You're just doing it all in software and commodity optics. And so you no longer need this 25,000 dollar optics package when you might be able to use the optics from a 200 dollar phone. It's really transformative when you can apply advanced software. The same applies to the factory as well. We have companies like Hadrian that are transforming the way high-mix, high-rate-of-production manufacturing is happening. When you say high mix, high rate of production, like what we think of as an old assembly line stamping out these little precision parts and then someone's putting them together... That's low mix. Low mix means you only make one thing. High mix means you make lots of different things. If you think about the factory of the future, right now these factories of the future have robotics and machines, but increasingly they will become driven by robotics and you can imagine that overnight a factory could reconfigure itself to make something else the next day. All the robots will just reconfigure their software to redo some other process the next day. Today, to reconfigure a factory is a massive disruption; you're changing the way the lines work and changing the way the supply chain and inventory gets managed. But the factory of the future may not be that way. I think the factory of the future just needs incredible ability to carry a lot of weight on the floor and a lot of power. Right now factories have power to power the machines, but you actually think about the factory of the future and you gotta charge all the robots up every night and when they're not working. So it's like electric vehicles. You're going to need all these charging stations. Your loading dock is not just for bringing in the semi-trucks; the loading dock now is a charging station for all the trucks and vehicles and forklifts. As VP of Tech at Amazon Business, tell us what does AI-powered procurement actually look like when it's working and what is the biggest misconception that leaders have about it? I think we skipped over when we were talking about whether venture can and should be in the business of doing like really expensive, heavy, dangerous things. You are on the board of a nuclear company. A lot of talk about nuclear now, particularly as there's like massive energy shock coming in from Iran but also just a huge giant sucking sound coming from AI off the off the grid. Where do you see in 5 or 10 years? Is nuclear like a real meaningful part of the new build in the US? I think there is... I think that is the case. On the just on the Iran energy shock, there was an article in the Wall Street Journal today, I think from the editorial board, about thanking Paul Ryan for allowing the US to export crude, which has actually softened the blow of rising oil prices and stabilized prices much more than they would be otherwise. That said, there is this insatiable thirst for energy; I talk about this all the time. It doesn't matter if it comes from solar, from fossil fuels, or from nuclear. But the thing about nuclear that I think people don't realize because it was maligned for so long was that one, the number of people who have died from nuclear accidents in this country since the beginning of nuclear capabilities, I think it's approximately zero. And as a result of that, I think we have just widely misunderstood... by the way, the people that die from coal mining, the just the long-term consequences of burning fossil fuels has got to be in the millions of people. So we're like zero people have died versus millions of people have died. So I think that people no longer see it as a threat. And then the question is, can we actually generate nuclear power economically? And the answer to that is largely by I think AI will play a role in that. The regulatory hurdles for nuclear are very, very onerous. There's a tremendous amount of paperwork and permit process, some of which is justifiable, but AI can actually rapidly, rapidly decrease the time to apply for regulatory permits, for nuclear fuel permits, for transport permits, for manufacturing permits. And then on the government side, the AI can actually help them process the applications much more quickly. And that's actually happening today. And so you know, we are seeing the supply chain for nuclear fuel be resourced domestically. We are seeing the manufacturing of nuclear facilities be shored up domestically. We now have a factory in Oak Ridge, Tennessee for Radiant Nuclear that's making nuclear power plants. These are transportable and they fit in a shipping container kind of thing. And then we see large-scale nuclear being done and all of them are very, very good. We should be doing all of them. There will never be a point in the United States where we have too much power generation. That will never happen. It's like compute, it's like storage. Why Scale Will Not Solve AGI | Vishal Misra - The a16z Show

a16z · 2026-03-17
Vishal Misra (Professor, Columbia University)
> "Anthropic makes great products...
TL;DR: While LLMs are mathematically precise Bayesian inference engines, they are limited by their inability to learn continuously (plasticity) and their reliance on correlation over causation; true AGI requires a shift from pattern matching (Shannon entropy) to causal...

TL;DR: While LLMs are mathematically precise Bayesian inference engines, they are limited by their inability to learn continuously (plasticity) and their reliance on correlation over causation; true AGI requires a shift from pattern matching (Shannon entropy) to causal simulation (Kolmogorov complexity).

The LLM as a "Giant Matrix"

  • Mathematical Abstraction: Vishal Misra describes an LLM as a gargantuan, sparse matrix where every possible prompt is a row and the distribution over the vocabulary (e.g., 50,000 tokens) forms the columns.
      • Sparse Distribution: Because most token combinations are gibberish, the matrix is extremely sparse. LLMs act as a compressed representation of this "true" probability distribution.
  • Bayesian Updating: In-context learning (ICL) is the process of the model updating its "beliefs" in real-time.
      • Evidence-Based Inference: As a user provides examples in a prompt, the model shifts its posterior probability distribution.
      • Domain-Specific Example: Misra proved this by creating a cricket-specific language (DSL) that the model had never seen; by providing few-shot examples, the model’s probability of producing the correct "nonsense" tokens moved toward 100%.

The "Bayesian Wind Tunnel" Experiments

  • Proven Precision: To counter critics who argued "anything can be Bayesian," Misra’s team created a "Bayesian wind tunnel"—a controlled environment where models were given tasks with analytically calculable solutions.
      • Architectural Performance: Transformers matched the precise mathematical Bayesian posterior down to $10^{-3}$ bits of accuracy.
      • Hierarchy of Architectures: The research found that Transformers are the most capable Bayesian engines, followed by Mamba. LSTMs were only partially successful, and MLPs (Multi-Layer Perceptrons) failed the tasks entirely.
  • Geometric Signatures: By examining the gradients of frontier open-weight models, Misra found that the same mathematical geometries observed in small experimental models persist in massive production LLMs.

Fundamental Barriers to AGI

  • Lack of Plasticity: Unlike the human brain, which remains plastic throughout its life, LLM weights are frozen after training.
      • Memory Limitations: While LLMs "learn" during a conversation context, they "forget" everything once the context window is cleared. AGI requires "continual learning" without "catastrophic forgetting."
  • Correlation vs. Causation: Misra argues that deep learning is currently trapped in "Shannon entropy" (predicting the next token via association) rather than "Kolmogorov complexity" (finding the shortest causal program to explain data).
      • The Simulation Gap: Humans use causal models to simulate the world (e.g., dodging a thrown pen). Current LLMs only match patterns and lack this internal "simulator."
  • The Consciousness Debate: Misra rejects the idea of LLM consciousness, describing them as "grains of silicon doing matrix multiplication."
      • Objective Functions: Humans are driven by "survival and reproduction," whereas LLMs are driven solely by "next-token accuracy."

The "Einstein Test" for Intelligence

  • The Manifold Limitation: LLMs are excellent at navigating existing "manifolds" of data (the space of human knowledge). However, they cannot yet create *new* manifolds.
  • Theory of Relativity Challenge: Misra proposes a benchmark for AGI: train an LLM on physics data only from before 1911.
      • The Goal: If the model can independently derive the Theory of General Relativity—rejecting existing Newtonian axioms to create a new representation of space-time—it has reached AGI.
      • Current Failure: Current models treat anomalies as "noise" or outliers rather than using them to synthesize a more efficient, shorter "program" (Kolmogorov complexity) for the universe.

Notable Quotes

"Anthropic makes great products... But they are grains of silicon doing matrix multiplication. They don't have consciousness. They don't have an inner monologue... You can rule out their consciousness." — Vishal Misra
"To get to what is called AGI, I think there are two things that need to happen. One is this plasticity... Secondly, we have to move from correlation to causation." — Vishal Misra
"Einstein had to create a new manifold. If you just stuck with the old manifold of Newtonian physics, you would see these correlations, but you could not come up with a manifold that explained them." — Vishal Misra
"I think deep learning is still in the Shannon entropy world; it has not crossed over to the Kolmogorov complexity and the causal world." — Vishal Misra

Causal models are the ones that are able to do simulations and interventions so you know Judea Pearl has this whole causal hierarchy where the first hierarchy in the first hierarchy is association which is you build these correlation models. Deep learning is beautiful, it's extremely powerful I mean you see every day all these models are like amazingly good. The current architectures don't do that. Another example I think which will make it clear is the difference between I'll use these technical terms Shannon entropy and Kolmogorov complexity. Sure. So if you look at the Shannon entropy of the digits of pi it's infinite, it's impossible to predict and learn what digit will come after, so that's the definition of Shannon entropy and Shannon entropy sort of tries to build a correlation, it tries to learn the correlation, deep learning does the Shannon entropy. Kolmogorov complexity on the other hand is the is the length of the shortest program which will reproduce the string that you that is under question. Now the program to get the digits of pi are very small, thanks to Ramanujan and others you know there are all sorts of really small programs that can reproduce it exactly. Creating prediction markets (and suing the CFTC) with Tarek Mansour and Luana Lopes Lara

Stripe · 2026-03-17
Tarek Mansour (Co-founder and CEO, Kalshi) Luana Lopes Lara (Co-founder, Kalshi)
Kalshi is the first federally regulated prediction market in the US, achieving 11x volume growth in six months ($10.4B in February 2024) after successfully suing its own regulator, the CFTC, to permit election markets.
TL;DR Kalshi is the first federally regulated prediction market in the US, achieving 11x volume growth in six months ($10.4B in February 2024) after successfully suing its own regulator, the CFTC, to permit election markets. By leveraging a decentralized network of...

TL;DR

Kalshi is the first federally regulated prediction market in the US, achieving 11x volume growth in six months ($10.4B in February 2024) after successfully suing its own regulator, the CFTC, to permit election markets. By leveraging a decentralized network of "super-forecasters" rather than traditional institutions, Kalshi is evolving from a binary "yes/no" platform into a full-scale financial exchange for non-traditional commodities like GPU compute and weather events.

Regulatory Strategy and the CFTC Lawsuit

  • "Permission First" Philosophy: Unlike the traditional Silicon Valley "ask for forgiveness" model (e.g., Uber, PayPal), co-founders Tarek Mansour and Luana Lopes Lara pursued a regulatory-first approach, spending four years securing CFTC approval before launching.
      • The "Pocket Veto": In late 2022, the CFTC delayed election market approval, leading to significant layoffs and a collapse in investor morale.
  • Suing the Regulator: In a rare "anti-pattern" for a 25-person startup, Kalshi sued the CFTC in 2023 after the agency blocked election contracts for a second time.
      • The Legal Basis: The court ruled that elections have clear economic impacts and do not fall under prohibited categories like war, terrorism, or assassination.
      • Outcome: The victory allowed Kalshi to list 2024 election markets, which became a primary driver for mainstream adoption and institutional credibility.

Market Mechanics and Participant Profiles

  • The 95/5 Liquidity Split: Contrary to rumors of "Jane Street" dominance, less than 5% of Kalshi's maker orders come from large institutional market makers.
      • "Internet Anons": Over 95% of liquidity is provided by individuals and small "garage" shops (roughly 2,000 active full-time entities) who monitor specialized situations in real-time.
  • Super-Forecasters vs. Experts: Kalshi’s most accurate data often comes from non-traditional sources.
      • Case Study: The platform’s top inflation forecaster is a resident of Kansas with no prior financial background who simply "feels" the data through obsessive news consumption.
      • Niche Monetization: An Ariana Grande super-fan profited $150,000 by trading on music chart rankings, using the proceeds to pay off student loans and buy a car.
  • Incentive Engineering: Kalshi uses fee structures to incentivize "pro-social" market behavior, rewarding those who provide stable liquidity during volatile events (like touchdowns or breaking news) to prevent spreads from blowing out.

Hyper-Growth and Product Roadmap

  • Growth Numbers: Volume reached $10.4B in February 2024, representing an 11x increase over six months.
  • The Four Pillars of Expansion: To become the "biggest derivatives exchange in the world," Mansour and Lopes Lara are focused on:
      • Breadth of Topics: Moving from politics and sports into "commodities of the future" like GPU compute and shipments.
      • Market Structure: Transitioning from simple binary (Yes/No) contracts to complex futures, swaps, and options.
      • Margining Systems: Moving away from 100% upfront capital requirements to allow for more capital-efficient hedging on long-term events (e.g., hurricane insurance).
      • Distribution: Expanding the "broker mix" through partners like Robinhood and Interactive Brokers to reach retail and international users.

Social Impact: An Antidote to Polarization

  • Truth Incentives: Prediction markets are presented as a corrective to algorithmic social media feeds. While social media rewards engagement and "rabbit holes," Kalshi rewards accuracy and "skin in the game."
  • The "Iowa/New Hampshire" Effect: Prediction markets create a real-time narrative based on probability rather than punditry. This provides a faster feedback loop for policymakers to understand how specific messages or events are actually resonating with the public.
  • Insider Trading Policy: Kalshi maintains a strict surveillance division to flag trades based on confidential data.
      • Stricter than SEC: While members of Congress can legally trade stocks, Kalshi prohibits them from trading on political contracts related to their own legislative work.
"We wanted to build the next generation New York Stock Exchange. We wanted to build a financial market that is in the US, that is credible, that is regulated. We were not very excited about this idea of building something offshore." — Tarek Mansour
"The law applies to companies, but it also applies to the government... The lawsuit told the CFTC that they couldn't just do whatever they wanted." — Luana Lopes Lara
"The incentive structure for a prediction market is truth. It is more volume, it is more liquidity, which translates to a better and a more accurate forecast." — Tarek Mansour
