You still need the commentators, but they're going to be able to show a number and be like, "This is what the forecast is and this is my opinion on it." I don't think the opinions are going to disappear. You guys referenced insider trading earlier, and there's just the policy question as to what the right policy should be around insider trading when it comes to prediction markets. I think it's pretty nuanced. Like, it's nuanced from the stocks case, right? Where famously there's lots of—you know, you see SEC enforcement actions all the time against things that aren't allowed. But there are cases like a hedge fund having proprietary satellite data of the Walmart parking lot and using that to trade earnings. That is information that only that hedge fund has, but you know that is permissible. And so similarly, I think there's just a complex set of line-drawing exercises here where presumably we don't think government officials should be trading in advance of military actions. What about leading up to the Super Bowl, you know, predicting the Bad Bunny halftime show length? People have that information, and so where do you think the lines should get drawn on insider trading? Yeah, and that is just—you said it perfectly. It's a very complicated question. And it's a complicated question for stocks way more and to a bigger scale than it is with prediction markets. The line that we take now is that we follow what the federal law is. So it's basically if you have... and these are, sorry, CFTC rules? CFTC and SEC, they both have it. So basically, if you have signed an agreement that says you cannot share some part of data—so if I work at the Bureau of Labor Statistics and I have in my confidentiality that I'm not able to say what the inflation number was before, then you cannot be sharing that data. But if you know that they're going to be rehearsing the Thursday before the Super Bowl and you're outside and you're like, "I hear Lady Gaga singing," that's fine. And that's the same thing that a lot of hedge funds do with, like, Starbucks, and people know that, okay, there's more people or fewer people in the store. And that's the point. It's like markets are very good at incentivizing information. We want information to come to the markets, but we don't want it to be unfair. And if you have access to it in an unfair way, you should not be trading on it. Okay, so you cannot trade on information where you have some duty to hold that information confidential. Right. We actually take it even a step further. For example, if you are a government official, you cannot—if you're in Congress—you cannot trade on bills passing, even though I don't know if they have an agreement. Famously, Congresspeople can trade on stocks. Right, right. So it's like we are actually taking a step further there. We're working a lot with the regulators because obviously it's a very new problem for them and for us. But we have an entire part of being regulated; we have an entire surveillance division that is looking at every single flag. They pretty much don't sleep, and they just try to figure out everything. And we put out two cases two weeks ago of two insiders that, because we're also regulated, we're able to charge them a lot of fines. So we charged them over five times what they made and all those things—they're banned and all of that. What was very interesting to me about these stories is that you guys were doing that, where with the public equities markets, the SEC is extremely enthusiastic about enforcing their insider trading doctrine. Just what has the CFTC been like on this topic? It's a great question. So obviously, with the CFTC you can think about it as three steps, right? The first step is our own surveillance and enforcement, then the next step it goes to the CFTC and their own surveillance and enforcement, and the last step it goes to the Department of Justice. Yep. And I think that the biggest difference is when people look at the SEC cases, most of the time they take a long time because of the research and the investigation. They put some fine, they block someone, and it kind of goes through the process. So it's still like that. Every single trade on Kalshi goes to the CFTC; they have every single thing. Every single case goes to the CFTC for them to review. So they might take action, we don't know, but now... Okay, so you refer cases to the CFTC? We refer cases to them. But we kind of do our first step of our first level of protection there. Yeah, yeah. I'm curious; there are clearly markets where nobody knows the answer yet of some event in the future. So, like, it's sort of impossible to insider trade. And then there are markets where a single person can change the outcome—like the mention markets in a speech, or maybe a sports player doing a specific number of shots. So how do you think about kind of that spectrum? Like, are mention markets a bad idea because they're just so inherently gameable? Yeah, or are they like limited in scale fundamentally because... the mention markets like the Brian Armstrong Coinbase earnings thing and stuff like that. Worst example. But yes. I think that, well, inherently I think mention markets are actually great. If you think about the Fed, right? Like the amount of hedge funds that are just sitting down being like, "Is he going to tilt his head this way or this way?" and "If he does that it means he's not very sure," so it's... Fed meeting minutes are the original mention markets. Exactly. That's actually pretty much it, and it was because we just know that specific words being used mean very specific things and it can move the markets so much. The same thing with Trump, right? If Trump says, "We are going to war," that's going to move the market a lot, right? Or if he says things like "tariff" that everyone knows moves the market a lot. Even in just a lot of everywhere, things that people say move the market and move a lot of different things. So I think the mention market is very important. Obviously the person that is working on the speech or that is saying the speech cannot trade. And that's kind of how we enforce it: if you are Gavin Newsom and there's a market on what you're going to say, you cannot trade it and your staff cannot trade it. That's part of the political cuts that we do there; they cannot trade. I think what you're saying is social media feeds ultimately try to resolve up-front. They're like, "I need to figure you out—are you a Democrat, are you a Republican—what post should I show you?" and pretty quickly you end up down a particular rabbit hole because they have pigeonholed you. And you're saying prediction markets do not have that phenomenon. Instead, there's a bit of a reverse phenomenon. It's like, "Are we feeling too certain about this person?" and I think that depolarizes things because the dimension is not one-dimensional anymore, which is like Republican-Democrat. And that's what you're seeing and what you're hearing about now. It's like, "Well, this guy's kind of cool," or "This guy might do good in Texas even though he's a Democrat." And you know, the same thing happened with the New York mayor situation, right? Everyone's like, "Cuomo's going to win 100 percent, 100 percent Cuomo's going to win, there's not even a chance," and we were just seeing Mandani's odds going up. I think the progressive message was really working with New Yorkers, and the markets were really seeing that uptick. That decrease in polarization really comes from people taking a step back and being like, "What do I actually think is going to happen? I'm incentivized the right way." It's the Iowa, New Hampshire effect here where, you know, in presidential elections in the US, there's some big name leading into the election—you know, maybe it was Hillary Clinton in '08—and then Iowa and New Hampshire are measurements of the sentiment of those two states, but they also create narrative. And I think what you guys are saying is prediction markets create this Iowa, New Hampshire effect where they can create narrative in a way that changes the ultimate outcome. I wouldn't say it changes the ultimate outcome. I think it sheds light into what the ultimate outcome is going to be. Potentially changes it. Well, I think... there is clearly some reflexivity. I mean, there's always some, but it's like with the polls too, right? Yes, I mean there's polls... Aren't you guys hiding your lamp under a bushel here? You're like, "Well, we're not changing anything here." You're just like... No, no. Prediction markets are a big deal. It's okay to say they'll change things a little bit. High odds don't always correlate to a good outcome. So you saw Mandani, when his odds were 94 percent on Kalshi, the thing that he was measuring pretty consistently—and I think there was a little bit of worry there—is like, "You've got to show up," right? Because if you have very high odds, that could also lead people to be like, "Okay, this is decided." So it's not as clear, and I don't think this changes things more than polls change things. Does that make sense? So the response is more like, in a vacuum, yes, if you had nothing else—if you didn't have social media, if you didn't have news, if you didn't have any of that, yes. But because we have all these other things and you add a prediction market to it, the impact is there. But I want to make one point that I think is actually very interesting, and we're seeing this often... We'll be the judge of that. Okay. You can judge that. Let me know if it's interesting. But a lot of times when you see people start participating in prediction markets, they get more engaged in the underlying. They legitimately just get more informed. It pulls people in. It pulls people in, but to do research. Now, because you have some skin in the game or you're about to put some skin in the game, the way you read everything changes. You're not just saying something crazy on Twitter anymore; you're putting money. And now it's like, "Let me read, let me actually figure out, oh, who's this person, what's happening?" It's like, "Are they pro-this? Are they against this? What's their view on everything?" And you go, and because—you know, it's amazing because this happened a bit in the New York race, or it happens a bit with Brexit. People voted for Brexit because, "Oh, we want Brexit," and after they voted for it, they're like, "Oh god, wait, wait, I don't think we wanted this. Wait, wait, we didn't even understand what we were voting for," right? And I think this heightened engagement engages people further to learn and understand. And you know, in sports, basically if you ask a lot of the leagues, they will tell you the same thing where people got more engaged with the statistics: which player is good, what's happening? And I think that will happen—is starting to happen and is already happening extensively—in politics, which is a good thing. I think that politics is going to get better because you're going to have a way faster feedback loop on the messaging and the policies. Right now, when a candidate says ten things and they win or they lose, you're trying to make one assessment of so many things that the candidate did. Did that go well or not, and which point was it? Even if you do a poll, it's always delayed. But now you can have real time: they said this, what was the response? You can get that faster feedback loop, which I think makes startups great. You're able to iterate very fast, and I think if candidates are able to optimize their message to what really people want and what policies people want, I think they're going to end up being better because they're just going to know what people want better. It's like you get a score on all the different things you've done, not one score that encompasses the good and the bad. Which is very much like when you ship a new feature: you're able to have, like, ten metrics and you're like, "Okay, this went up but this went down." Markets can kind of contribute to that, but also to a lot of other things as well, like music with charts. So when someone listens to a song and they're like, "Definitely not going to hit number one," you're like, "Okay, that was that song, so we should do something else." Do you guys try to use prediction markets in any way internally to make decisions? Every single decision we make is always probability. Even the election lawsuit, right? When we're doing it, it's... But that's you guys evaluating the probability. But do you ever think about creating markets for your employees to participate? So we have one nit. Yes. Which is, as a regulated exchange, we can't trade. Yeah. There's like a separation of church and state thing there. Yeah. Exactly. And so we've been asking—we're working with the regulators—could we do something small? Like, could we do small dollars so that we can... because obviously that's one that we really want to. Can't even dogfood the product, I guess. And dogfooding the product is a big deal. Sorry, employees cannot trade in their personal capacity? They can't. Not at all. Interesting. But yes, that makes it very hard when you, as you say, just can't dogfood your own product. People at Facebook use Facebook, and that's how you make the product good. Right, so that's why it's so important for us to just be asking the users all the time. Yeah. It sucks. But the power users, the super-forecasters, are a lot of what influences where it goes because they're very engaged. You guys must presumably spend a lot of time with those power user super-forecaster types and just have them on speed dial. They're there day one. So, you want to make sure they're happy. Last question: where do you guys want to see prediction markets policy go? Like, when you're talking to someone in government, or if you had a magic wand, what are you arguing for? Our stance as a company—and I think this may differ a little bit from, I would say, your average tech company or big tech company—is that we are pro-innovation. Innovation needs to happen in America. You know, we have to lead and we have to do it right, and we have to win. Like we have to be all the things that Americans want to do, or we need to win as a country, we should have it here. But we're also pro-regulation. And at a higher level principle, there's usually this tension that generally policymakers want to regulate and the tech companies don't. I think you're maybe more like a traditional financial firm in that way, right? Where maybe Silicon Valley firms grew up in an unregulated way, but financial firms have always had a regulator and that's just a fact of life. It's just a constraint to work on. So it's part of the culture, and I mean, again, we spent four years getting regulated upfront, so it's part of it. But we believe in regulation. I think it's important because regulation is a bit like insurance. It's protecting you from things going wrong in bad times. And so when I think about where this lands, I think anything that is oriented around preserving these in America and making sure we win, but then elevating the fairness and transparency of the markets, is right. Anything that's oriented like, "How do we make it more fair, ban insider trading, and add more restrictions on, for example, government officials and members of Congress trading on information they shouldn't trade?" I'm obviously a big fan of banning insider trading from members of Congress. That's... we talked about that, yeah. Presumably you mean just banning trading period for members of Congress. I think it's not a bad idea, but I think things around creating fairness and transparency—because of all the questions that we asked—if people are basically trading on politics, let's have all the trade data be as public as possible so anyone can audit it. Anyone can see it, which is a good thing, right? Now you don't know—imagine a poll where the general public can check every single person that was polled and what the sample was like. And then anything around customer protection. Because that is important long-term in the sense that, when you build a consumer product and it goes mainstream, there is a massive burden on the companies to educate. You've seen it over and over, and in our case, you want to make sure that people know what they're getting into, they're not overextending themselves in terms of how much they're trading, and they're not getting into an area of discomfort. We can do as much as we can on the marketing and on the product side, but we need policymakers and regulators' help to basically make it an industry standard and help us elevate ours. And by the way, we're pro that. I think that even the classic retail brokerages should also be adopting a lot of these customer protections that we're talking about, which they don't. I think that every retail trading platform should be taking a lot of these steps. So, yeah, I mean that's sort of our general view, and we hope that this is sort of the direction that things take because there can be a variety of views, right? Some people believe that any type of speculation should be banned, whether it's in the stock market or crypto or prediction markets. We don't believe that. We think that would be a bad outcome because there's a lot of upside to having liquid markets in a variety of different things. But also, because if you ban it, you're actually heightening the risks that you're trying to prevent, because now that activity is going offshore where you cannot monitor it or police it or do anything to protect it. Awesome. Well Tarek, Luana, thank you guys. Thank you guys. I think what Matt's getting at is like, who is a market maker on Kalshi? Like, you know, there's all these Jane Street conspiracy theories. How to bet on yourself (without venture capital)

Invest Like The Best · 2026-03-17
William Hockey (Founder, Column)
William Hockey, founder of Column and co-founder of Plaid, argues that the modern venture capital model encourages "safe" entrepreneurship and consensus-driven thinking.
TL;DR William Hockey, founder of Column and co-founder of Plaid, argues that the modern venture capital model encourages "safe" entrepreneurship and consensus-driven thinking. By self-funding a nationally chartered bank and focusing on the "boring" infrastructure of the global...

TL;DR

William Hockey, founder of Column and co-founder of Plaid, argues that the modern venture capital model encourages "safe" entrepreneurship and consensus-driven thinking. By self-funding a nationally chartered bank and focusing on the "boring" infrastructure of the global US dollar, Hockey advocates for a high-risk, high-conviction model of specialist building that prioritizes long-term profitability and national security interests over the "VC hamster wheel."

Rejecting the "VC Hamster Wheel"

  • The Heroin of Venture Capital: Hockey describes VC funding as a "heroin-like" addiction where founders optimize for the next fundraise rather than long-term product viability. This leads to "windy" business paths where strategies shift to follow trends like AI or stablecoins just to secure capital.
  • Extreme Founder Risk:
      • Hockey funded Column by pledging over $1 billion in Plaid shares to secure a $70 million loan to buy a bank, nearly facing bankruptcy and margin calls three times in the process.
      • He argues that Silicon Valley has de-risked the founder role too much, attracting people who want the "founder" resume item without the "raw intensity" of potential failure.
  • Employee Equity and Liquidity:
      • Annual Tenders: Column uses 25% of its earnings every year to buy back shares from employees, providing predictable liquidity that traditional startups lack.
      • Anti-Dilution: Because the company avoids external funding rounds, employees face zero dilution, making a 1% stake in Column significantly more valuable than 1% in a heavily diluted VC-backed firm.

The Strategic Moat of "Boring" Problems

  • The Specialist Advantage: Value is found in deep, unglamorous niches that "Gemini deep research" cannot solve. Hockey emphasizes becoming the world’s leading expert in "confusing, boring-sounding" areas.
      • Extreme Research: Hockey studied 2,000-page histories of 19th-century Japanese and Chinese banking to find single product ideas that create millions in leverage.
  • Software Company + Bank: Column is a software company that owns its own regulatory rails.
      • Unlike traditional "BaaS" (Banking-as-a-Service) providers that sit on top of other banks, Column is the bank.
      • 90% of revenue comes from software fees (API calls), not the interest rate "spread" typical of traditional banking.
  • Customer Longevity: In financial services, customers like Ramp, Brex, and Wise pay for safety and longevity. Profitability is a feature that proves the company can be a "15-year partner" without relying on a VC’s investment committee.

Emerging Markets as Innovation Labs

  • The Silicon Valley Consensus Problem: Silicon Valley and Beijing are the two most consensus-oriented places in the world. Elites build software for other elites, losing touch with how "everyday America" or the rest of the world operates.
  • The "Kinshasa" Perspective:
      • Hockey frequently visits emerging markets like the Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC) to observe how founders build under extreme constraints.
      • Leapfrogging: Just as China skipped laptops for mobile, emerging markets are leapfrogging Western financial services. For example, Kaspi in Kazakhstan and Rawbank in the DRC offer integrated vertical apps (paying taxes, renewing licenses) that far exceed the utility of US banking apps.
  • The Dollar as a Global OS: The US dollar is most critical in "implicitly dollarized" emerging markets where local central banks are untrusted. These regions often need US financial infrastructure more than developed European markets do.

The US Dollar as National Security

  • Sanctions Before Missiles: Hockey views the US dollar as a primary national security asset. Controlling the global financial OS allows the US to enforce its will through sanctions—collapsing an enemy's trade ability—before ever putting "boots on the ground."
  • Infrastructure vs. Implementation:
      • Contrary to the narrative that US financial tech is "built on cobalt," Hockey asserts the Federal Reserve's underlying technology is actually quite robust and capable of 24/7 instant settlement.
      • The friction in the system (e.g., banks being closed on weekends) is a result of business model constraints (staffing, fraud protection for the vulnerable) rather than a lack of fundamental technology.

AI and the Future of Value Accrual

  • Distribution Over Technology: Value will not accrue to "AI companies" but to those with massive distribution and brand moats.
  • The Beneficiaries:
      • Inefficient legacy brands with high headcount costs are the biggest potential winners of AI, as they can use the technology to drastically cut operational costs.
      • Fraud Detection: AI’s greatest contribution to fintech will be matching human-level fraud detection instantly. This will allow banks to remove the "protection friction" that currently slows down money movement for everyone to protect a small percentage of vulnerable users.
"I think the most important thing you should do is say... what does my margin profile look like? What is my revenue growth over time? And how should I find a capital structure that matches that? The venture model is built off the fact that you can make a company like an ATM... that’s not the case with most markets." — William Hockey
"Silicon Valley is not a popular place... our ability to actually build software or have ideas or perspectives that really resonate is probably at the low point in the entire time I’ve been here. Elites end up building software for elites." — William Hockey
"I think that the good founders bet on themselves and take an extreme amount of risk... When there’s literally only one door in front of you, you don’t have a choice. You have to go in. And that fear creates another part of you." — William Hockey
My guest today is William Hockey, the founder of Column. William was also the co-founder of Plaid, one of the more famous fintech businesses from the last decade. Column is his second business, which he’s built from scratch, funding it entirely himself and building it his way. I think you will find this conversation utterly fascinating, not just because of the incredible quality of the business that he’s built, but how maniacal he is about studying and implementing ideas in this specific field. This is a great example of a founder that is winning because he is willing to do everything. My favorite example from our conversation today is that he went and found some obscure book about some ancient bank in Japan and found one idea buried in the 2,000 pages that gave him a simple idea for his product. He’s willing to do that over and over again, and he explains his very different, maybe even heretical, views on a lot of what’s happening in the world of startups and technology today. He offers a very different way of building that I think will be inspiring and interesting to those that want to build a company. Please enjoy my conversation with William Hockey. Usually, I don’t start with a description of the company that someone’s building. But in your case, one, I don’t think a lot of people are yet familiar with Column, and I want to fix that. It’s such an interesting beast in and of itself that it’ll be our excuse to talk about many fascinating things in the world. Can you just start by explaining what the business is and does? At a high level, we are a software company that also owns a bank. What we do is we say, okay, we have this interesting regulatory moat with a bank that most other people don’t have, and we’re going to build just incredible software behind it that nobody else can build because they’re not a bank. We started out by serving a lot of software companies that want to get into financial services in the US. We provide the backend infrastructure that powers the payments, deposits, and credit of amazing companies like Built, Wise, Ramp, Brex, and Mercury. These types of companies run on our software and then our regulatory rails. And then we also expand that to anybody wanting to do things with a global dollar. That could be international fintechs. A lot of times, it's global banks or banks in emerging markets that need to transact and hold things in the dollar. In the US, you have vertical software people building business software. This is an area that is probably going to get changed as AI kind of rolls through. People need to go deeper down into the business. Just building software for software's sake is not the case anymore. Now, people actually need to control the underlying finances of the business. The Brexes and Ramps of the world have proven that you can actually build enterprise software that also touches the money. In order to do that, you actually have to control the dollar. You have to control the money, whether through lending money, holding credit, etc. And so we just expose a set of primitives and APIs to allow anybody to do that super easily. Could you maybe pick a customer that people might recognize and describe literally what services or products they use? How do they pay you for those products or services, just to really nail it home? Maybe I’ll use a company out here that recently relaunched, a company called Built, which maybe a lot of New Yorkers have, or a lot of people in cities have. If you look at the card on the back, it says, "issued by Column." We’re the ones that are actually connecting with the networks, managing the networks, and we’re actually the regulated entity behind that. Then, when you need to go pay your rent, or your landlord is going to deduct money from your Built account, if you look at it you’re like, "Oh, the account and routing number there, that’s actually a Column account and routing number." They build the application, they build the website, and they build the consumer marketing. We’re going to handle everything behind the scenes that has to deal with the Federal Reserve, or TCH, or the card networks, or SWIFT. We’re the ones that kind of build the software for that and handle all of that complexity. We are technically a bank, but unlike banks, we make 90 plus percent of our money off of software. Similar to any kind of SaaS company, it’s a per API call. It’s a pure-play tech business, and then we pass most of the economics from the actual bank side of the business down to all of our customers. One of the things I love whenever we talk is you’ve always been somewhere strange and interesting. Kinshasa, I think, was the last time we were together. I don’t know a lot of founders going to Kinshasa very often. Why are you so often in interesting, kind of bizarre locales around the world? This is my second company. I started Plaid back in 2012. It’s very easy to stay in Silicon Valley. The quality of life is amazing, there’s a lot of money to be had, and there’s a lot of super smart people. But you can start to get quite isolated, and you can start to get very consensus focused. Probably a lot of your listeners read Dan Wang’s last letter on China. He has this great and I think accurate but somewhat harsh criticism, where he says the two most consensus societies he’s ever been to are San Francisco and Beijing. I think that’s quite accurate actually. San Francisco is probably the most consensus place I’ve ever been to. I think that is both a huge clutch for us, but it’s also probably our most valuable asset. Because as a founder, if you’re building in something like AI, or stablecoins, or something that San Francisco believes is very consensus but the world does not believe yet, that’s actually a great operating environment. You can go and you can have these outlandish ideas that other people are going to believe in, that nobody across the world would believe in. You can build this in a very safe way. I think that’s why Silicon Valley and San Francisco are so dynamic and we’re so in front of the curve. But we also have completely lost touch with how the rest of the world operates, or even how everyday America operates. You’ve probably seen this kind of smack us in the face over the past decade or two. It’s very important to go to places that don’t have that same bias. If you think about emerging markets specifically, the founders who build there are just everyday people. They live in this constrained society. They’re constrained in a way that San Francisco and New York isn't. And that breeds a different type of creativity. It breeds a different type of innovation that you really can’t get anywhere else. If you go talk to people in London or Vienna or Mexico City or San Francisco, people are living, to an extent, in a world of abundance. That causes a very specific creation cycle. Why if you go to Kinshasa, which is the capital of the Democratic Republic of Congo, it’s going to be the largest city in the world in probably 5 to 10 years. I think it’s already larger than most of the megacities. Probably 95% of people in Silicon Valley couldn’t tell you what Kinshasa was the capital of. But there are tens of millions of people that live in a highly, highly constrained society. That breeds a sense of creativity. It breeds ideas and stuff that you can’t really get anywhere else outside of an emerging market. So that’s one. I think second, for my business, the dollar is fundamentally global. The dollar tends to be strongest in places that you could imagine are relatively dollarized. Places that are dollarized tend to be more emerging markets, where they are using the dollar as their main currency, either unofficially or officially. Maybe they can’t trust their central bank, or they have a history of super bad inflation and the country got implicitly dollarized. Those places tend to actually need US financial services more than, I don’t know, the UK where the GBP is pretty strong, or France. Those places don’t need American financial services as much as maybe some parts of the emerging world do. So sticking with Kinshasa as an example, you go there, what are you doing there? What are you discovering? They operate in a world where there’s actually relatively large markets. DRC, to take that as an example, is one of the largest exporters, if not the largest exporter, of some critical minerals in the world. So there is a lot of money flowing through there. It’s a massive exporter. It’s a place where there’s a lot of Chinese investment. Africa broadly has had more Chinese investment than anywhere else in the world outside of Pakistan. So there is money and there’s a lot of people doing things. The population growth is absolutely bananas. I mean, the population growth in Africa is probably larger than Western Europe, North America, and parts of Asia combined. While their GDP per capita might be quite small, there’s still a lot going on. And where there is, there are founders that are building super cool things. The large companies actually tend to be quite innovative, and I can talk about that in a second. So I talk to them, I meet a ton of people, I’m meeting CEOs of the largest multinational companies there. I'm meeting founders on the ground, and I’m talking them through like: what are you building? What is your perception of America? What is your perception of American financial services? How can we be helpful? And you just, honestly, I spend a lot of my time just walking around, kind of ideating, just taking in the scenes. Sometimes, a quarter of the time, I come up with a really interesting idea that ends up building us a cool product or just a good market. I probably add 90% of my ideas either in the shower or walking around in a random emerging markets country. It kind of expands your senses a little bit. If I’m walking down the Marina Green or I’m walking through the Mission in San Francisco, the only thing I’m thinking about is how is AI going to change things. Because you can’t walk around San Francisco and just not get completely hit with AI FOMO 24/7. But there’s other stuff we need to do in order to get people up to mobile penetration. Take DRC; mobile phone penetration is still less than 25%. Banking penetration is still less than 5%. That's crazy. There’s stuff we need to do before we think about embedding LLMs into everybody's brain. If that penetration is that low, will you and your business naturally benefit from that going up based on the products that you’re building? Is that how you think about some of these opportunities where it’s much lower hanging and just no one’s paying attention? The leapfrogging that happened in Asia is obviously quite well known. China skipped the laptop and went straight to the mobile phone most famously. We skipped online e-commerce and went straight to social commerce in China. There’s going to be leapfrogging as well. And you’re going to see the same thing in financial services. Financial services tend to be most innovative and most progressive in the worst countries. You can see this in Argentina, you can see this in Iran, and you can see this in other places. Iran’s financial system, say what you will, it’s complicated. They have to deal with a lot of incredible constraints. Thus, they have built a lot of bespoke stuff just for themselves because they do not have access to global financial markets. And when you get to design things from scratch, you end up actually building things a little bit differently. That’s actually quite interesting. If you look at these emerging markets, take Africa for example, they were the first ones to do mobile payments and M-Pesa decades ago, well before Venmo. If you talk to them, they are actually quite a bit more open and they are used to their category being somewhat disrupted. They also have an interesting thing: they have access to differentiated talent. If you are in the US, pardon the banks I’ll shit on here, but you do not have access to the top talent. The top talent’s going to Anthropic, they’re going to Google, etc. But if you believe that brains are distributed equally, you go to Congo and there’s going to be some proportion of equally smart people as there are in France. They don’t have an Anthropic to go to. They don’t have the ability to move to London and go to DeepMind. But there’s still a pretty decent talent pool there. They’re going to go to where there is job safety and where there is money. That tends to be, in a lot of emerging markets, the breweries and the banks. That’s where the money is. And so the talent at the middle and top can actually be quite a bit higher than people think. I’d say you take an emerging markets bank executive team, they’re hands down way better than what you see in the Western world. They also have the ability to verticalize much better than they do in the US. We already have amazing software and amazing retail experiences down the entire stack. In most emerging markets or non-developed countries, everybody has a bank account, or a lot of the people who have a phone have a bank account, so they can actually cross-sell there effectively. I think one of the most interesting companies out there is Kaspi in Kazakhstan. They started out by buying a bank, and then they just kind of did everything. They’re the largest e-commerce company and the largest bank. You pay your taxes on Kaspi. You renew your driver’s license, it’s on Kaspi. They realized where people start is in financial services. If we acquire financial services, we can cross-sell and we can distribute products there. The largest bank in Congo is a bank called Rawbank. Highly sophisticated; you download the mobile app, and it’s way better than anything we have here in the US. You can upgrade your TV subscription on it. Imagine JPMorgan doing that, or Bank of America, or Wells Fargo. Or quite frankly, even US fintechs doing that. Even if they could build that, there’s no market for that. And so their ability to land and expand is fundamentally different. The good thing for us is, in all these countries, the main currency is the dollar. Our ability to innovate with that is much more akin to what a fintech looks like in the US than maybe a large traditional bank. Do you end up earning similar amounts of revenue from outside the US as you do inside because of these potential relationships? The US market is so good and fintech is so developed. One of our theses is that fintech is probably going to be the last area that is somewhat disrupted by AI. I think what you’ll see is a collapsing of domestic fintech and enterprise software. You already see this with the Ramps of the world as they go deeper into the workflow management side. Some of our customers are growing just so quickly. But the rest of the world is also a big part of our revenue. It’s something that we’re super excited about as well. It could be both Western Europe or emerging markets. Can you say more about this comment that Silicon Valley, along with Beijing, is the most consensus place? As you traipse around California, what strikes you as the strangest? You’re building something so different and you spend so much more of your time away from there. You’re able to get outside perspective despite being of that place originally. What stands out as the strangest elements of it and its culture today? I’m a product of Silicon Valley. I’ve been there since I was 21, started some of the top companies in Silicon Valley. But as you get older and as you travel more, I think you have the ability to look back and be more retrospective on the society you grew up in. SF and Silicon Valley are an elite-dominated society, whether we like it or not. It’s probably more akin to Wall Street in the 1990s than it is to what we want it to be, which is a research lab in Cambridge in the 1950s. Maybe that was Silicon Valley in the 90s, but it’s not anymore. What happens is elites end up building software for elites. I think that has somewhat made sense because people buy something that’s aspirational and then it moves down market. But when you do that, you can start to drink your own Kool-Aid a little bit too much. I think that has probably happened in Silicon Valley. We talk to each other, we build for each other, and we think that the market is each other, but we don’t actually look broader than that. And the companies that figure that out do really well. If you look at AI, our research labs are doing fantastic. That’s a consensus-oriented problem. You take a bunch of people that are super smart and you pretty much blind off everything from them, and they can all talk and share ideas. That’s a fantastic research place. We are going to win on that alone. But as you think about applicability to people’s everyday lives, people in Silicon Valley don’t live everybody else’s lives. They don’t live the lives of everyday Americans; they don’t live the lives of other people outside the world. Our ability to actually build software or have ideas that really resonate is probably at the low point in the entire time I’ve been here. Silicon Valley is not a popular place, and I think we tend to forget that. We think we’re on the top of the world, but I don’t know what our approval ratings are. I think they’re probably pretty dang low. And I think that’s for good reason. It’s something that I at least try to focus on a lot. I have to build software and I have to build products that are applicable to outside the walls of San Francisco and New York. But that’s probably less and less the case. Most software companies try to maximize your time on their app to juice engagement. Ramp does the exact opposite. Ramp understands that no one wants to spend hours chasing receipts, reviewing expense reports, and checking for policy violations. So they built their tools to give that time back, using AI to automate 85% of expense reviews with 99% accuracy. Since Ramp saves companies 5%, it’s no wonder that Shopify runs on Ramp, Stripe runs on Ramp, and my business does too. To see what happens when you eliminate the busy work, check out ramp.com/invest. Every investor should know about Rogo. Rogo AI’s platform is not just another generic chatbot. Instead, it was designed to support how Wall Street bankers and investors actually work, from sourcing diligence and modeling, to turning analysis into deliverables. For me, three key things differentiate Rogo. First, it connects directly to your systems, so it can work with your actual data. Second, it understands your workflows—how work really happens across a deal or an investment. Third, it runs end-to-end and produces real outputs the way the best people do. It produces auditable spreadsheets, investment memos, diligence materials, and slide decks that match your standards. This all comes from the fact that Rogo is built by finance professionals for finance professionals. It’s already being adopted by some of the most demanding institutions in the world. To learn more, visit rogo.ai/invest. OpenAI, Cursor, Anthropic, Perplexity, and Vercel all have something in common. They all use WorkOS. To achieve enterprise adoption at scale, you have to deliver on core capabilities like SSO, SCIM, RBAC, and audit logs. Instead of spending months building these mission-critical capabilities yourself, you can just use WorkOS APIs to gain all of them on day zero. That’s why so many of the top AI teams you hear about already run on WorkOS. WorkOS is the fastest way to become enterprise-ready and stay focused on what matters most: your product. Visit workos.com to get started. One of the things that you’ve done that’s so unique is not raise money. But you built at a pace and a scale that looks as though you raised a ton of money. Your cap space is huge. Why did you choose to do it this way in the first place? I started Plaid and I did the standard Silicon Valley playbook. You ideate, you build stuff, you put a deck together, and you go to 80 venture capital firms and hopefully one of them gives you money. Then, every year, you move up the alphabet. I think that worked, and Plaid is a very successful company. I’m very lucky to have started it. But Silicon Valley sometimes gets confused. You’re either a venture-funded company—meaning you're ambitious and you're going to build amazing software—or you’re a bootstrapper. If you're a bootstrapper, you write thought pieces on Twitter but you hire subscale people and grow small amounts for a cute lifestyle business. I think you can actually be highly ambitious, hire the world’s best talent, and build a massive company without being addicted to venture money. What we do is we say, okay, we are going to grow by our earnings. We’re going to make sure that 100% of employees and myself own the company for the foreseeable future. It makes it a lot harder and it puts some constraints on your business. But not only for the net worth of myself and my employees, but also culturally, it’s actually quite a bit more effective. Being long-termism is a little bit of a trite saying, but I think it does actually help us. Silicon Valley companies are probably more long-term oriented than your average business in the US, but the hamster wheel of VC doesn’t actually allow us to be long-term. If you are spending a lot of money and burning at a rate that requires you to raise every year or year and a half, you end up optimizing for that next fundraise. You say, okay, stablecoins are cool this year, so I need a stablecoin strategy. Okay, AI is cool this year, so I need an AI strategy. Your business might be going in the general direction, but it’s taken a pretty windy way to get there because you need capital. What you are doing is very rational, but that’s not going to be the straightest line to your goal. I sometimes make this joke: VC money is kind of like heroin. It feels good, it’s amazing, but you’ve got to keep shooting up. It’s very challenging to get off. I know very few people that have tried heroin once and they’re like, "Oh well that was awesome, I’m done." How many people do you know have raised a $100 million Series A and then said, "I’m done"? It just doesn’t happen. If you raise $100 million, you should be able to build a billion-dollar business. Why do you need to keep raising it? There’s a lot of structural reasons for that. San Francisco is a bit like a factory by design, and I think that can be quite effective. But I think for the ambitious founder, I actually don’t think it’s the right thing. For us, I can invest in things that I think are going to have a 10-year payback and I’m totally fine with it. If we grow 80% versus 110%, that doesn’t really matter that much. As long as we’re profitable and as long as we’re building the software we want, I just don’t have the same constraints. That is freeing in a way that most companies can’t operate. What’s an example of something that you’ve done or made that long-term investment in that you think you wouldn’t have done if you had been venture-backed? I mean, we bought a regulated bank when we were early. I think that’s probably a little bit cooler now in this new administration, but we bought a regulated bank during the first Biden administration. That required us to not grow or not focus on revenue for two or three years and do a bunch of stuff that inherently didn’t scale. Buying a bank is not a software-defined problem. That’s not a great use of venture capital dollars that you expect to grow. That was a relatively non-consensus bet at the time. There’s just no way that could have happened. There’s no way somebody would have been like, "Oh, we want to fund this type of company." They'd want you to do stablecoins or AI. That’s just not a fundable thing. To be able to just invest behind that for a multi-year period before we actually take on clients, people think they’re long-term focused, but they're just not. I think we’re able to also do weird stuff with employees that doesn’t scale. Such as what? We pay employees like $2,000 a month toward their rent or mortgage if they live within two miles of the office. That doesn’t inherently scale, but it’s massive. Now I can get people who live close to the office; they feel great because they’re getting a huge part of their housing stipend in San Francisco subsidized. Is it a good use of venture capital money? Probably not. We can do all this stuff from an employee perspective, from a retention perspective. Every single year, we take 25% of our earnings and we just buy back our shares with employees. We just run our own tender every single year. It’s been great for retention, able to actually get liquidity to employees when they need it. It allows people to believe that we’re in this venture growing business. If you’re in a VC business, they tell you to use 100% of that money for growth. We say we can afford it because we're profitable, so we invest in growth but also in employees. Are you granting equity to people in a similar way that a normal startup would, vesting over some period of time, and then just every year saying we’ll buy back some portion of that to provide liquidity? Yeah, structurally we look exactly the same as a high-growth startup. We go after the same people and we have all the same perks, if not more. We operate very much like a high-growth Silicon Valley startup. We happen to just also be quite profitable. What I tell people is: imagine our profit every year is our funding round. On January 1st, we raise a massive round each year. That’s how we view it. There’s part of it that’s going to be used as a tender to buy back company shares, and the rest of it we’re going to put to growth. Oh, by the way, you also haven’t diluted at all. You also don’t have any pref stack. We get to make our own decisions. That’s super compelling. I can hire someone and say, "Over a 10-year period, you’re not going to be diluted." If you’re an early-stage founder, you’re going to lose probably 50 to 75% of your equity value due to dilution alone. You may lose 10 to 80% of your upside due to the preference stack. These are weird economic things that people don’t really understand until they’ve been doing this for a long time. With us, we say, "Hey, just don’t worry about that stuff. What I give you is what you’re going to have." By the way, you have liquidity on a yearly basis going forward. That’s super compelling to people. How have you honed the communication of that idea to a new employee so that they get it? We’re far from perfect. I think one of the things we do is we target people for whom this is their second company. We are probably not the best place for a new grad to land. A new grad is going to go through a calculation of where all their friends are going, what the number one company on Hacker News is, and what the "thoughtfluencers" are talking about on Twitter. That’s not a bad strategy if you're a new grad. But you aren’t really going to be thinking about some of this nuance. It’s actually quite refreshing. We go to people and say, "We are super fast growing, but we provide you yearly liquidity. Here’s some very basic math on preference and dilution, and here’s how you can make way more money for the same equity value." It’s a hard story for a 21-year-old, but it’s an easy story for a 25-year-old that’s been through six rounds and four pivots at their previous company and has nothing to show for it. Does this show up in employee retention numbers versus Silicon Valley norms? We have almost close to no regretted attrition. The people that we’d like to stay end up staying. I think there’s probably a couple reasons for that. Second time around, it’s a lot easier. I learned from a lot of mistakes at Plaid and I know where to spend time and where not to. We’re probably better at picking talent and we know what matters to people. Sometimes, as founders or VCs, we think that people join companies because they want to become billionaires. Maybe that’s true up to a point, but a majority of people join companies because they hit their late 20s or early 30s and they start optimizing for something else. They want to send their kid to a good school. They don’t want to live with roommates in a one-bedroom apartment until they’re 35. Housing and education are super important, and you can’t feed that on illiquid stock. So how do you think about optimizing for that? You think about basic things like team and culture. People want to work for somebody who makes them feel taken care of—not just over a 20-year period. They want to know: do they understand my short-term needs? Can you take care of me in the short, medium, and long term? Sometimes we are too long-term oriented. We say, "Work on this for 20 years and you’re going to be a decamillionaire." That resonates with a certain type of person, but it doesn’t resonate with everybody. Building a culture that optimizes for every single point of an employee’s journey is quite unique and leads to really good numbers. How much of this was just possible because you were already rich at the start and had money to fund something like this? Is this portable advice? I think it’s hard. It was definitely successful at first. But what I would say is, I started a very large company, but I think I was probably less liquid and less rich than everybody thought at the time. Plaid’s a funny story. We attempted to sell to Visa for $5 billion. That’s kind of the point when I left to go start something. It didn’t go through. We got blocked by the DOJ. I did not sell my company, thus I did not have any money. My liquidity versus paper wealth was pretty extreme. I got a lot of credibility from people being like, "Oh, this guy’s rich, he’s good for it." But I did not have any money. I pretty much funded the entire company with debt. I went to a bunch of banks and I said, "Here’s a bunch of Plaid shares, please give me money." The best I got was a SOFR plus 10% loan at 5% LTV. I pledged over a billion dollars of stock to get $70 million. I bought the bank for $70 million. I haven’t had a lot of money in my bank account for a long period of time. The business became profitable, and I got to pay off that loan over a period of time. But in the process, I practically got margin called three times and almost went bankrupt multiple times. Talk about that stress. The first three years were definitely the most stress of my life. I had a fundamental thesis that we could pull this off and build a business that 100% of employees and myself owned. But to do that in a world where you need to invest and not make money for multiple years is very, very challenging. The regulatory climate at the time was intense. I have this loan I have to pay off. It was probably the most intense period of my life. As a founder, you have to shelter that from everybody. You need to be transparent and bring people in, but you also need to not bring people all the way in. I had pretty extreme conviction in myself. I thought I knew 100% that over a multi-decade period I could pull this off. But there’s that queasy quote: "Markets can stay irrational longer than you can stay solvent." It’s kind of true. I’d look at that quote and wonder which side of that equation I was on that day. We ended up making it through, but the idea of "billionaire buys thing and self-funds it" is further from the truth than people think. What’s it like getting margin called? If you owe a bank a million dollars, you have a problem. If you owe a bank a billion dollars, they have a problem. Margin lending in private companies is not a great business because when you want to take collateral that is stock, that’s exactly when you do not want to hold that private stock. I have immense appreciation for the people that did it for me. I’m not quite sure they got a great deal out of it. The reason I feel the story is important to tell is because that's where so much of the value gets created—those extreme entrepreneurial risks. How did that experience affect you? I think that good founders bet on themselves and take an extreme amount of risk. The extreme amount of risk part is something that we no longer have. When there’s literally only one door in front of you, you don’t have a choice. You have to go in. That fear and that innate desire creates another part of you. It creates creativity, it creates inspiration; it’s an extremely valuable part of the founder journey. In many ways, I think in Silicon Valley we’ve actually removed that. We’ve created this environment where it’s really safe to start a company. There’s a playbook: you go through YC and, assuming you’re moderately competent and went to the right high school and college, you’re going to get a $3 million seed round. Worst-case scenario, you can go work at a great company as an engineer. You'll have "founder" on your resume and life is good. That has created a lot of value, but I’m not quite sure it’s created a lot of great founders and companies because there is no risk in that proposition. If you go back to pre-2008, you were on the edge of the knife. That creates intensity and creativity and fear that is a critical part of the founder journey. I don’t know why we don’t talk about it more. We don’t create environments where a founder has to bet on themselves. If we did that, we’d actually be in a slightly different place. I am always somewhat perplexed by second-time founders who have made a bunch of money. Of the $100 million they’ve made, they’re putting a million dollars of their capital at risk and they’ve raised $500 million. Why? If you believe in this so much, why aren’t you going all in? If I’m an employee, I look at them and think, "You're asking me to go all in but you won't." The weird thing is an early-stage employee takes way more risk than an early-stage founder. Explain that. Let’s talk about an example. I’m a 24-year-old. I’m making 400K or 500K at Google or Meta. I’m going to go to an early-stage company and get 1% of the company and make $90,000. I’ve now changed the trajectory of my life. I can no longer buy a house; I can no longer go on the vacations I want. I’m making a trade-off where I’m going to make pretty much no money over the next four or five years, hoping that maybe in five years I’m going to make millions of dollars. That’s actually a lot of risk. I’m now going to live with my friends instead of living by myself. But as a founder, you're not making those changes. It’s a much higher likelihood that at the next round, regardless of your company, you’ll be able to sell some secondary. If it shuts down, you can go be employed at a great company because you have CEO on your resume. That first employee at a failing company, that’s actually not a great resume line item. We’ve de-risked the founder but we haven’t de-risked the early-stage employee. I just think we need to increase the risk for founders. We need to make failure much more expensive. If you’re a second-time founder and you have liquidity, put your money into that. If you’re going to be asking this of employees, you should ask it of yourself. Starting companies is just too fucking safe. That's caused a lot of companies to be super safe companies. We’re going to pivot to AI and wrap OpenAI or Anthropic. That’s not bold. It’s because we’re attracting founders that actually maybe just want to be employees. They don’t think about the long term. They don’t say, "If I don’t pull this off, I’m going to become bankrupt." That’s when you bring out the rawness of humanity, and I don’t see that very much anymore. How have you felt that in yourself? I am not the most diversified person on the planet. I own two things: I own Column and Plaid. That’s it. I don’t even own a majority of my house. That is truly it. Those are the only two things. And that’s motivating to me. I have a six-month-old son and I do need to diversify at some point. As a 36-year-old, you should probably not be this concentrated, but it’s also what makes building companies unique. There's probably a lot of people that look at me and think, "Billionaire, amazing." But it’s illiquid equity. That’s what drives me every day. If you don’t have something you are driving towards, such as solvency, it’s really hard to be motivated every day. Compounding on yourself is probably the best investment you can make. I’m not a generalist; I’m a specialist. I’m probably the best in the world at a couple small, boring things. I’m really good at creating really confusing, boring-sounding companies that are really hard to explain on podcasts. That’s my expertise; that's my niche. I feel pretty confident in my business because I do not think you want to compete with me. I’m mean, I’m hungry, and I know my little niche space better than anybody in the entire world. If I’m investing in something else, there’s a me on the other side of that trade. I don’t want to do that. I’m a builder. In a world where it’s increasingly competitive, the world for builders is the world of specialists. You have to go extremely deep into your area. I probably have read more about the history of financial services than most. I studied banks in Japan in the 1800s. I read a very boring 2,000-page book on the history of banking in China in the 19th century. There’s stuff I got out of that. You probably get one small thing in a 2,000-page book. It’s not efficient unless you own a thing that happens to be in that space. That leverage on one little thing can create millions or hundreds of millions of dollars of value. That’s where you find your leverage. This notion of being the best in the world at the thing you do is really interesting to me. The environment today makes that harder than ever because there’s so much distraction. Spending your day reading about Anthropic is exciting. How do you become the best in the world at what you do? One of the best determiners for success of founders is: can they find the most boring thing humanly possible interesting? And can they find that interesting over a multi-decade period? Who doesn’t find AI interesting? Geopolitics is fantastically interesting. There’s all these general topics that are quite broad and mass-market interesting. That’s why Twitter is so fascinating—people like to feel they are really smart across a broad swath of categories. But that doesn’t really align to company building. So many people right now are thinking about how AI is going to disrupt software, how AI is going to disrupt vertical software, or how AI is going to be the next CRM. These are like generalist topics that I can probably find a thousand people that have like pretty like interesting compelling ideas and can go pretty deep on that. But you can’t create value there. You can create value if you’re like, "I am the number one person in the entire world at this little niche thing," and I think this niche thing can like can generate billions of dollars in revenue over time. But the problem is, is those places are really boring. Like the fun ones, like food and like surfing in Thailand or whatever, like those are solved categories. Like yes, I would also love to be an expert on like hospitality in like Thailand and Southeast Asia. Like that’s a fun problem. I could imagine one going niche on that for like a multi-decade period, but those are solved problems. But I think finding the extremely boring thing that requires you to read hundreds of thousands of pages that you cannot like Gemini deep research your way through, that’s where value is. But it’s fucking boring for a lot of people. Like you have to you have to suffer in silence for a huge amount of time. And if you can find that, you know, if you can find that like super fascinating, you can like love to learn that, then I think you’ll be successful. But I think it’s a it’s a minority of people. Like like my partner kind of gives me shit all the time but how on earth do you find that book interesting? Like what is wrong with you? And I was like, well like if I don’t, like you and I are going to be super poor. You said earlier that building a company for the second time is a lot easier than the first time. Yeah. What are the most extreme ways that that’s true? Like what are the things that you’ve done the most differently this time than the first time? Your experience is valuable. Like I started I started Plaid right out of college. And I think Zach, who is my absolutely incredible incredible co-founder, we both kind of said like man if we would have just like worked at a company for like nine months, we would have saved like three years. Because the amazing thing about working at a company, especially an early-stage company, is you just like you just get to fail forward all the time. And that’s like incredible, that’s an incredible lesson. But like when you fail forward as a founder, that’s a lot of dilution. That’s a lot of time. That’s a lot of like wasted resources. And if you could do that on somebody else’s dime, amazing. Now I think it’s like a little bit easier because like everybody’s YouTube videos on like you know YC startup school and there’s like a playbook and there’s like some PDF for everything and you can probably like Gemini your way through like the early-stage part of a company. But experience does matter a lot. What about picking talent? Like how how what things do you optimize for now that have been honed because of your prior experience? When people always think about employees and this like missionary mercenary framework, right? Which is like well like you have to look into an employee and like what like what do you want to do. There’s like the mercenary type, which is okay, super smart, probably super pedigreed. And like and really what they are doing is they are using your company as a launchpad for something else. They are using they’re using your company to like collect a bunch of two-year vested options from like the top five companies and like hoping one of them goes up. That can be a valuable employee but you have to have a very like specific type of company that is used to like that churn and burn in order to take advantage of them. Then you have like the missionaries, right? Which is hey like you this person is very mission-focused, they’re very like inspiration is super important to them, and if you get that right they will go to like the ends of the earth for you regardless of like their short-term benefits. These people also have some downsides as well, which is you know the moment maybe you want to be a little bit more commercially oriented or the moment you have to maybe make trade-offs on your mission or something like that, that can cause a lot of like societal unrest inside of your company. Right? And then there’s like the third category of employees, which are you know generally what I think are probably like the best, which is like there’s a kind of a combination of everything. I actually feel pretty lucky where, you know, at Plaid in the early days, we we actually had a pretty hard time fundraising. Our first round I think we probably chatted with like 80-90 investors and we got rejected. And so I like sometimes I think I have a reputation for being like oh like William’s like somewhat anti-VC. It’s actually like not further from the truth. I like Plaid would not exist without VC, straight up. I just don’t necessarily think like the VC model is perfect for every single type of company. Or like maybe like a VC owns like, you know, 10% of your company or 20% of your company and maybe the employees themselves own like 80%. Like like there are different cuts of this you can make. So I think figuring that out. But I think the most important thing you should do is say okay, like realistically what does my margin profile look like? What is my revenue growth over time? And what how should I find a capital structure that matches that? And am I actually going to grow at a consistent enough period? Do I want liquidity in 10 years that the venture that the venture model makes sense for me? Because the thing that people forget is yes growing 100% on a $10 million base or a $20 million base or a $30 million base, you know it’s not like it’s not like that hard. Everybody shares these graphs now on LinkedIn, look we’re the fastest company to get to $100 million like of all time. And like that’s awesome. But you know what’s like much harder? Growing like above 30% a year off of a billion dollar revenue base. That is like 99 times harder than going to zero and $100 million in three years. However, the venture model only works is if you can grow above 30% off of a billion two-billion dollar base. And you have to like really look inside yourself and be like does my model make sense for that. And can I do that in a way that I can ingest hundreds of millions if not billions of dollars of capital on the way and put really good use to that? Because I think sometimes businesses grow at the rate of the market or businesses grow differently than just dollar and dollar out. And the venture model is built off a fact of you can make a company like an ATM, which is you put in a dollar you get a dollar thirty back. That’s not the case with most markets. And so I think you have to look really intensely at yourself and your business to make sure that works. And and there’s like so many amazing tech companies that works for, and that’s why that venture is like a like a really good asset class and Silicon Valley is probably like the best place to accumulate capital of anywhere in the entire world probably throughout all history, but that’s not every single business. To see what happens when you eliminate the busy work, check out ramp.com/invest. Every investor should know about Rogo. Because Rogo AI’s platform is not just another generic chatbot. Instead it was designed to support how Wall Street bankers and investors actually work, from sourcing diligence and modeling, to turning analysis into deliverables. For me, three key things differentiate Rogo. First, it connects directly to your systems, so it can work with your actual data. Second, it understands your workflows, how work really happens across a deal or an investment. And third, it runs end-to-end and produces real outputs the way the best people do. Auditable spreadsheets, investment memos, diligence materials, and slide decks that match your standards.
