How this CEO rose to lead a $1.7 trillion financial giant

CNBC Television · 2026-03-17
Jenny Johnson (CEO, Franklin Templeton)
Jenny Johnson, CEO of Franklin Templeton, is transforming the $1.7 trillion investment giant by blending a multigenerational legacy of long-term stewardship with aggressive "native" adoption of blockchain and AI.
TL;DR Jenny Johnson, CEO of Franklin Templeton, is transforming the $1.7 trillion investment giant by blending a multigenerational legacy of long-term stewardship with aggressive "native" adoption of blockchain and AI. She emphasizes a leadership philosophy of "people, passion,...

TL;DR

Jenny Johnson, CEO of Franklin Templeton, is transforming the $1.7 trillion investment giant by blending a multigenerational legacy of long-term stewardship with aggressive "native" adoption of blockchain and AI. She emphasizes a leadership philosophy of "people, passion, purpose, and persistence" to navigate market volatility and technological disruption.

Leadership and Family Legacy

  • Generational Stewardship: As a third-generation leader, Johnson manages $1.7 trillion in assets—a massive leap from the $2.5 million firm her father took over.
      • She highlights that "third-generation failure" is common because of a lack of motivation; she avoids this by instilling family values like hard work and integrity.
      • External Validation: Despite being family, Johnson underwent a rigorous external board review before being named CEO to ensure she was the best candidate.
  • The "Four Ps" Philosophy: Johnson centers her leadership on four core pillars:
      • People: Selecting the right talent to allow the organization to scale.
      • Passion: Finding joy in the work so it doesn't feel like a burden.
      • Purpose: Rallying the team around financial milestones (e.g., retirement, education) rather than just beating an index.
      • Persistence: Staying the course through crises like the pandemic.
  • The Power of Mentorship: Her father, now 93, remains her primary influence. He taught her the importance of "taking care of the client" and the necessity of persistence, once using a cartoon of a frog refusing to be swallowed by a duck to illustrate the "never give up" mindset.

Strategic Execution and Crisis Management

  • Navigating the Pandemic: Johnson became CEO just before the COVID-19 lockdowns and concurrently announced the acquisition of Legg Mason—effectively doubling the company’s size.
      • Remote Integration: Due to travel restrictions, the firm had to conduct talent reviews and close the transaction via Zoom, ultimately finishing the deal two months early.
      • Early Tech Adoption: A prior culture of "desktop video" (using Tandberg devices) gave the firm a competitive edge in transitioning to remote work compared to less tech-savvy peers.
  • The "Long-Term" Advantage: Johnson argues that family-controlled public companies can resist the pressure of quarterly earnings to make "big bets" on technologies that may not pay off for 5–7 years.

The Technological Frontier: AI and Blockchain

  • Blockchain and Tokenization: Johnson is a vocal advocate for blockchain as a "source of truth" to replace aging financial infrastructure.
      • Cost Reduction: Blockchain eliminates the need for expensive data reconciliation between firms and counterparties.
      • Native Innovation: Franklin Templeton launched a money market fund built *natively* on-chain (rather than a "digital twin"), allowing for "atomic settlement" and interest calculated to the second.
  • AI as a Force Multiplier: Rather than fearing displacement, Johnson views AI as a tool to enhance human expertise.
      • Virtual Research Analyst: The firm uses a tool that ingests their investment philosophy and historical trades to act as a "bias check" during committee meetings.
      • Advice for Workers: She encourages employees to "own their careers" by becoming the most proficient users of AI tools, regardless of their seniority or coding background.

Career Advice and Productivity Hacks

  • Work-Life Balance (The "Five-Year Rule"): When faced with a conflict between personal and professional obligations, Johnson asks: "Five years from now, which of these things is going to be more important?"
  • Managing Jet Lag: Johnson’s primary travel hack is to sync with the destination’s time zone the moment she steps on the plane, avoiding sleep aids to preserve REM sleep.
  • Active Learning: She recently spent a weekend attempting to learn coding via Claude and Replit to better understand the technology disrupting her industry, underscoring the need for "curiosity and openness."

***

Notable Quotes

"Take care of the client and the business takes care of itself." — Jenny Johnson (quoting her father)
"It’s a little bit like when you drive a car... you don’t look right in front of the car, you look down the road. And when you look down the road, the car naturally stays where it needs to go. You’ve got to be focused on the longer-term picture while being aware of the noise in the shorter term." — Jenny Johnson
"It’s being willing as a family to put your own ego aside and say who's better on behalf of the family to be the steward of this asset." — Jenny Johnson
"The technology is not usually the limitation. It’s people’s willingness to change." — Jenny Johnson
Jenny Johnson is president and CEO of Franklin Templeton, a leading global investment management firm with $1.7 trillion in assets under management. She's a third-generation leader with a career spanning more than 35 years, working in roles across the company before taking the top job just before the pandemic. Today, she's pushing the company forward with big bets on AI, tokenization, and the future of finance. She centers her leadership on people, passion, purpose, and persistence. A CNBC Changemaker, Jenny Johnson is in the Changemakers and Power Players spotlight. Jenny, thank you so much for joining us for the Changemakers and Power Players podcast interview, and congratulations on being named a 2024 CNBC Changemaker. Well, thank you. It's a great honor. So Jenny, you are a third-generation leader of Franklin Templeton. You took on the role of CEO right before the pandemic and have had an incredible run, growing, expanding, and transforming your company. But this was a family business. Take us back to the beginning. When did you realize you wanted to join the family business? So my grandfather did start it, but I have to say that my father took it over when it was $2.5 million in assets and $10,000 in revenue. So he's the one who really built it, and today we're $1.7 trillion. I'm one of seven children and I was probably the only one that always wanted to go work in the business. I have no idea why, but it sort of was just something that I always thought I would do. There was one time in college where I thought, "Oh, I really like coaching, maybe I'll do that," and then I came to my senses. I decided, well, I could volunteer and do that and I'll stick with working in the family business. And so this was the thing you always wanted to do and you had all these other siblings. Was there a sense that it was a competition between you, or did none of them care for it and you really wanted to? What was that dynamic like? First of all, we all went and worked for the business at some point because you needed a job. So that was common. It wasn't really a competition. You know, the business was growing so fast when we were all sort of graduating from college. This was in the '80s; it was the heyday. People woke up to mutual funds at that point and you just had massive growth. So there was a lot of opportunity for everybody. There were some that were passionate about it and others for whom it was just the convenient job. And the ones that were passionate really stayed involved in the business for a long time. But so you didn't just have your career—I mean, obviously you did the rounds and experienced every part of Franklin Templeton—but you did go out and forge your own identity as a leader even before you came back and created your identity as a leader within the company. Was there a moment where you had a conversation with your family where you're like, "This is a company that I not only want to work at, but I want to lead"? It's interesting. We are a public company, but the family still has a large portion of the stock. I think it's really important when you're working in a family business that you stay in your lane. And I think all of us had looked at this gift that our father had given us in this ability to be a part of something that was big and exciting. And so my lane was in operations and technology, and I loved that. My brother was much more on the investment and distribution side and he became CEO. He then had an opportunity to go run the San Francisco Giants. I do think that there's a lot of people who think running a sports team is more fun. I personally prefer what I do. And at that point, it made sense for me to come over and be CEO, although it was not a fait accompli. The board put me through an external review to compare with other potential CEO candidates. And so I think that it was more about what can I do to help build this business, this family legacy. You certainly feel that as being part of it. Yeah, so when you were little, was this the dream? I never, it's funny, I actually never saw myself because I'm number six of seven as being the CEO. I just saw myself as being part of it. I didn't really think about it that much. And then when the opportunity came, I was thrilled to be able to do it. So I'm curious what it was like to work at all these different parts of the company before you became CEO. How did you find that experience of really learning not just one part of the business or the leadership of the business, but the meat and potatoes of the business? And how did you forge your identity as a leader through that? I think one of the challenges if you come and start your career with a big company is you become very narrowly focused right away and so you don't get broad exposure. I look at my dad. When he took over the company, it had only a part-time employee and then him. He's done every job. He's been the fund accountant, the technology, the client service, the investment person, the sales guy. At 93 years old, he will circle a footnote on something and send me a note and ask me a question about it, right? He understands it at that level. So I probably don't have that extensive of detail, but because I ran technology, because I ran the operational world, and I ran distribution, and the investment teams reported to me, I got to understand every part of the business and really do a deep dive. Although I have often said that the two things that were probably the most valuable in my job as CEO today: one was running technology. In a world of AI and tokenization, having that background is really important. But the second thing was at one point I ran a subsidiary, it was an auto financing business, which we sold off after 2008. But when you're CEO—and when you're CEO of a small company or CEO of a big company—it's kind of the same questions with more zeros. You're deciding how to allocate capital; you're hiring talent, right? So that was just great training for this job. And did you feel when you were doing all those different roles leading up to the CEO role that you had to prove yourself because of your connection to the family? Oh, for sure. My father said, "You work harder because everybody will look at how hard you work and they'll work just a little less." And with the expectations, you're setting the standard. And so there was no question; you had to earn it and it was expected. There's so much conversation about family-run companies and how they manage different situations, the good scenarios, bad scenarios. There have been TV shows like "Succession" about it, endless movies. When you look at other family businesses—and by the way, Comcast, which previously owned CNBC, is a family-controlled company—there's so many different examples. Do you look out there and say, "This is an example of a company that is family-run and I admire," and then "This is one that is what I want to avoid"? So it's interesting, there's a saying that you hear kind of globally which talks about the third generation. In the US, we say "t-shirts to t-shirts in three generations." In Northern Europe, they say "clogs to clogs in three generations," and in Asia, they say "rice paddies to rice paddies in three generations." There's a reason that is said, right? It's hard. Meaning that the second generation has big success and then by the third generation it's gone? Yeah, or the second generation saw how the first generation built it and was part of building it. The third generation has a really comfortable life and it's hard to get motivated to work as hard because you have all these other things that you could do and they're not going to necessarily change your standard of living. That's my speculation on why often it fails in the third generation. So actually, for my dad's 80th birthday, we hired an expert. She had been an estate attorney and she said, "I got tired of building all these estate plans and then having complete destruction in the heirs. So I wanted to understand what makes those families, you know, the Rockefellers, the others that have generation after generation success in maintaining something." And it was a couple things. One, it was the family has to have a set of family values that you instill and you say, "If you're part of this family, you live by these values." Hard work, work with integrity. My father always said, "Take care of the client and the business takes care of itself." So that was one. And the second thing is you actually have to decide who's the best talent in the family to be a steward of that asset. I jokingly say at one point I was on the board of the San Francisco Giants baseball team where my family is involved. My brother now runs the team. And I go, he is a much better steward of that asset than I ever was, right? He's just the right family member for it. So it's being willing as a family to put your own ego aside and say who's better on behalf of the family to be the steward of this asset. Wow, that was an incredible meeting and conversation. So you mentioned that there was an external review process before you were named CEO. What was that whole experience like? Because I want to go to that moment where you were named CEO just a couple months before COVID hit. Yeah, so I was named CEO. The day I started as CEO, five days later we announced the Legg Mason transaction. So that was doubling the size of the company. So you know, that was actually definitely a challenge for the board. Boards do not like to change CEOs during a transformational acquisition. And so it had been because I'd been in the company for a long time and they knew that I knew the company well that they had the confidence to do that. But you know, as you can imagine, that's a big deal. Two weeks after that, we had the two-week, remember, it was going to flatten the curve lockdown, and then that turned into kind of a two-year deal. You just manage it. Like these things come to you and you're figuring out how to manage. And we all learned so much during that time. I think the key is being curious and being open to really learning. We had to close a transaction where we couldn't even visit people, right? One of the most important things you do in an acquisition is talent review. And so we ended up having to do a lot of that via Zoom or Teams. And we ended up closing the transaction two months early. So that, you know, was good. Do you think that the fact that you ended up closing the transaction early was because of COVID? Do you think in a weird way COVID may have given you an advantage or benefited the clarity around that transaction? I think it probably did. Now there could have been missed opportunities in that, although I think because if you actually look at the transactions that we've done, we really have been focused on fielding the best team. You always know that you're going to have to reduce the combined workforce. The lost opportunity is to just assume that you have the best talent within the acquiring company. So you have to be open enough to say we're going to broadly look at it and decide. And again, best talent is really best team that you're putting together. And we ended up bringing on a lot of the Legg Mason talent. But you had to assess that talent through some of the information they had with HR, but also through video, which is not the same as working in person. On the other hand, you're not coordinating everybody's travel schedule, somebody's plane doesn't get delayed, like there are a lot of efficiencies too that probably played into it. Yeah, so you're named CEO, there's that lockdown, you're in the middle of this transformational acquisition. In that moment where there's so much uncertainty, what was your biggest fear as an incoming CEO? It's funny, I wasn't afraid. There were people who speculated, "Oh, should we have done this deal?" because remember the market took a hit, right? You know, "Oh, could we have gotten it cheaper?" It didn't matter because in the end we knew it was the right transformational move. And I had the confidence to be able to lead. I was well known in the organization. They had put me through—I mean it was interesting, there was some concern I think probably at one point on the board about whether or not the investment folks, since I wasn't coming from an investment background, would follow me. I had led them for a couple years. It came out, and they did surveys, that I had tremendous followership with them. So there wasn't really anything I was afraid of. And I looked at it and I said, "You know, you've got to manage through whatever comes to you." That something else will happen, we'll just manage it. That's an incredible sense of serenity at a time where so many people were freaking out. What would your advice be to people—new in a new job, in an old job—when they're faced with that kind of crisis and they need to get through, right? That's all anyone needs to do, you just need to get through. What's your advice to staying calm and confident despite that kind of volatility? Well, I will say one thing. When I had taken over technology—Franklin has clients in 150 countries, we have offices in 30 countries, over 100 offices—and when I was running technology I kept saying to the team, "We've got to have desktop video," because we have too many people that call into phone calls because we can't all be co-located. And we really pushed it. And at the time, desktop video was a separate device; it was called a Tandberg device. And so we had actually built in our culture a comfort around running meetings where somebody was on video. And you do have to do it a little differently. You can't talk over each other; you have to make sure you hear the people calling in on video. And so, first of all, I was very comfortable with that kind of running of meetings and things, and so that gave us a little bit of an advantage. I would say your advice is: look, there's going to be short-term factors that you have to adjust to—stay open—but it doesn't change your long-term goal and picture, right? And so just stay focused on the longer term. It's a little bit like when you drive a car. You don't realize it, but you do tell—if I were to teach my children how to drive the car, right—you don't look right in front of the car, you look down the road. And when you look down the road, the car naturally stays in where it needs to go. It's a little bit when you're running a company. You've got to make sure you're focused enough on the longer-term picture while being aware of what's going on with the noise in the shorter term. So do you think that you're able to be more focused on the long term because there is this family history? Oh, for sure. I actually think there's a huge advantage. And it is one of my worries about public companies is there's so much pressure on quarterly earnings. I look at the work that we're doing in blockchain. Like, we knew when we were making these investments—we didn't know how long, and I still don't really know how long it'll take before it's meaningful. But I am absolutely confident that blockchain technology will be critical to financial services at some point. Is it five years? Is it seven years? I don't know, but it will replace the infrastructure. And so why can we afford to spend the money on it? Because I look at it and I have to think in terms of leaving the company for the next generation. I've been trained to think that way. That doesn't mean the next generation will lead it, but it means being a steward of that asset for the next generation. And so the investments we made in this space where we're now real leaders, I think, are partly because of that view of that lens of thinking longer term. I want to get into blockchain, but first I want to dig into more about you as a leader. You're a long-term thinker, it sounds like you're a calm in the storm or a calm in the COVID crisis. How do you think about your leadership superpowers? What are they? I always, when people ask me, "How do you think about leadership?" I always say it's my four Ps: people, passion, purpose, and persistence. And I think it kind of describes how I think about leadership. I say, you know, the number one decision any leader makes is who are the people that you surround yourself with. Because you'll never scale if you don't have great people. So that's number one, is your people. Love what you do, it doesn't feel like work. I love what I do. I am passionate about what I do. Speak about it in a purposeful way. So you know, at Franklin Templeton we help people achieve their most important financial milestones in their lives, right? And so many goals have a financial component. And so it's really important, that rallies people around. Like if I'm just saying our job is to beat the index and show, you know, best risk-adjusted return, that's not that exciting. But if you say, "Hey, we're helping people so they can retire or they can help their kids go to college," like that matters. And so that rallies people around the mission. And then finally I say persistence, right? Look, you're not going to get it right. Stuff's going to happen. COVID's going to happen, other things are going to happen. It's all about sticking to it, knowing what that long-term course is, that long-term vision is, and sticking with it in those difficult times. I understand you use a lot of sports metaphors when you're motivating your team. What is your analogy between sports and business beyond the obvious, and why do you like speaking in sports metaphors? I don't know. I did play sports. I think my Wikipedia page says I played college basketball—I didn't, I got cut. But I love basketball, and I did ski race in college. You know, I think because what is sports? It's a team sport, right? I mean it's a team. And when I say putting together the best team, let's all remember we can learn a lot about the dream team that didn't win the Olympics, right? You had all this amazing talent but they weren't a team. Like so your job is to pull people together as a team. As a leader, it's to coach them. To not only coach them about where to go, but to coach them so that you get the best of each person. And then it's about sometimes when you're down, right, you've got to motivate people to say that's okay, we're still on the right path and we're going to keep at it. And to know how to stay motivated so that everybody's coming in and is excited about being part of the team. So actually I always think sports is a great analogy to actually running a company. Where did your leadership philosophy come from? You have the idea of the four Ps and the sports analogies. Did you have a mentor? Are there business books that have really influenced you? Hands down it would be my father. He's the greatest mentor and leader, and anybody who ever worked for him absolutely adores him. I mean it was funny, I was sharing with him, I posted on LinkedIn his birthday—he's blowing out his candles on his 93rd birthday. The number of overwhelming comments that came from people who had worked for him to just wish him well and everything. And that's you know, whether honestly it's the shoeshine guy at the club who adores my father—my father remembers every year to give them, you know, baseball tickets—to government officials, senior business people who will say, "I loved working with him." He's just an incredibly well-loved person and deservedly so. He's incredibly kind, but he's also competitive. And most importantly, I think he's the one who taught me like you just got to stick to it. I one time had a really difficult business situation. It was when I was running the auto loans. And it was tough. And my father left on my desk a little picture—it was like a cartoon—and it was basically a frog getting swallowed by a duck, but the frog had reached around and grabbed the duck's neck so it couldn't swallow and it just said "Never give up." And I would say that defines kind of who my dad is. And but what about you? How have you evolved as a leader? Because you can't just emulate your father. You have to be your own kind of leader. Not only are you a woman leading in this very male-dominated industry, but it's also a very different time. Yeah. And I have said—and it's interesting because I also have said—like I don't know whether this job is the right for the fourth generation either. They'll have to decide themselves. And this job is very different than the job my father had. I'm on the road—I mean it would be hard for me to do honestly when my children were young—because I'm on the road. In the month of March, I will be home six days. Now, other times it's not so bad, but it is the type of thing when you are a global company, your clients want to see you. If somebody's going to invest billions of dollars with you, they want you to show up in person. And there's a lot of different sovereign wealth funds, institutions, individual clients. And so you know, I did have to evolve. I had to evolve in a much more ability to speak publicly—that wasn't something that my dad had to do as much. I had to travel much more than probably historically, and I had to speak about the macroeconomic environment in a way that he didn't have to do. So I think the key with being a leader is you've got to be a leader for your time and you've got to be able to evolve into that leadership into that role. And what's been the biggest challenge for you personally in growing into this role and being agile in this crazy time we live in? The funny part, like if you—people always laugh because if you get me talking about technology, I light up, I get really really passionate. The part that I had to learn—leadership, motivating people, any part of the business, making tough decisions—those are not really a challenge for me. I had to learn to be able to speak about macroeconomic events regardless of what they are, because that is expected when you're running a global asset management. And that wasn't an area of training. I was an Econ major but that was about it. And you have to be able to speak. I remember being on CNBC one time and they said, "Hey, we're going to cut over to the Beige Book and then we want your view." And I'm thinking, "Oh gosh, let me remember what the Beige Book was." Fortunately, I got to hear what the guy said so then I could talk about it. But that honestly surprised me initially is just the ability to talk about it and now I am very comfortable doing it, but it was an area that I did have to learn. Yeah, so it sounds like you get comfortable once you get your reps in. You mentioned AI and technology and your love in working in this world of technology. But you recently said that you see disruption in the software space as incredibly real and AI changing the role of financial services companies. What is the risk to financial services companies? Look, I think in any of these technologies—and by the way, this is the same in history—you have to understand enough about what the technology can do and then understand where you can add value to the technology versus being afraid that the technology is going to replace you, right? And the good news with technology, honestly, is that human nature—the number one issue when you have running an IT department is change management. People actually don't like to change. The technology is not usually the limitation; it's people's willingness to change. So I think the good news is it takes much longer to become part of a business and disrupt a business than in general people expect, and it's because of people. But you have to understand where it's going to change. So I look at it and I think, "Boy, if my investment teams aren't thinking about how they're leveraging AI to get more data, to consume it faster." We created in our solutions team a virtual research analyst where they essentially have fed in the philosophy of that investment team. It's looked at historical trades and it sits as part of the investment committee so that when they're making a decision it'll say, "Hey, by the way, our philosophy is X, do you have—have you considered these factors?" right? There's always bias. Human nature is biased. It doesn't mean it's making a decision, but it's going to make you better at how you approach it. So if we have people that are on the investment side and aren't trying to think about how to leverage these tools, that becomes a concern to me. If we have people on the distribution side that aren't thinking about how to leverage these for a client service, that's a concern. Yeah, so that's so interesting. And so how does your work in tokenization fit into all of this and building on the blockchain? Because I guess on one hand, if everything is moving over to AI, does that make tokenization and blockchain less important? No. So first of all, you have to think about—just blockchain is a programming language and it does three things really well. Number one, it has a source of truth. The second problem after change management in IT departments is the quality of their data, right? And why? If I go grab a piece of data and it's out of date, suddenly it's wrong. Or I go grab a piece of data and that wasn't the source of truth, it's wrong. So that coordination around data is actually a huge issue in IT departments. And the second thing is companies in the financial services industry spend a ton of money on reconciling data between systems. Blockchain is only one source of truth. So not only do I not have to reconcile that data within my own firm, but we don't have to reconcile that data with the counterparty because you also have to reconcile with your counterparty. It all goes away with blockchain and it's immediately up to date and current. The second thing it does is it has this ability to execute a smart contract. So whatever you and I agree in our transaction, it gets programmed into it. So I have comfort if you're selling me real estate, I don't have to go to the title company to validate that you have the rights to that real estate. It's actually embedded in that smart contract in the token. So we're going to be able to take a lot of friction and cost out of transactions because I can program that in. I know that you have the rights to the ownership because you have the token. And two, I know what the terms are going to be because they've been programmed in. That's hugely efficient. And when you add AI to that, the types of creativity you're going to see in the types of transactions and velocity of transactions is going to get really really interesting. And then the final piece is it has a payment mechanism. So once you and I agree, I have confidence that I'm going to get paid once the transaction—once you take my token, I know that I got paid. We use often a third-party intermediary because we don't really know who the counterparty is on the other side. And so we don't have to use that as long as we know when that transaction happens, I'm going to get paid. And there's atomic settlement; it means I get paid right now, I don't have to wait for a delay. And I know I just want to address one thing because people always raise this: "Well, what about AML and KYC?" you know, all that nefarious stuff. You take our tokenized money market fund—we KYC and AML the wallets, so it can never go into a wallet that hasn't been approved by us, even though it might sit in a Kraken or a Binance wallet after there, but we have control over that. Again, that's because this token is programmed to have that ability. So our money market fund today, number one, and we know how cost-effective it is because you eliminate all that reconciliation. We ran it parallel process. But you get paid in your account the interest every day. You have a money market fund, you accrue it, but you don't get paid till the end of the month when they give you the yield. In ours, we'll post that in your account every day. If you actually hold it—let's say you're holding collateral and you're a hedge fund and you want to do partial day collateral posting—you'll get paid. We calculate it every second and you get paid for the four hours, 32 minutes and 22 seconds. And it's because we are the only money market fund that's natively built on chain. The other ones are called digital twins. Digital twins mean that you still live within the confines of the existing system—you get none of the benefits of blockchain—and then it's copied on the blockchain. We skipped that step and we went straight to building it natively on chain. So we are really excited by both the ability to drive down cost and the ability to create new products in this environment. Yeah, so it sounds like it's a huge commitment of yours and also in a way sort of leaping ahead because you're so committed to this long-term plan—back to the long-term planning. Yes, definitely. But so back to what you said earlier about AI and the fact that yes, AI now is a big tool and it's like another advisor in the room among the voices that can be heard. But as AI continues to improve, do you see massive numbers of jobs being eliminated? So it's interesting. If you look in history, every time that there's something new, there becomes this tremendous concern that there's going to be massive unemployment ultimately. Now, the pace in which technology is adopted definitely can impact that. But I go back to 1997. The cover of Business Week said "the death of the broker." In other words, nobody was going to need a financial advisor because you were just going to use the internet and it was going to just do it for you and it was going to be robo. And today, your financial advisor does so many more things; they're not just an investment advisor. They're doing financial planning. In some cases they're—I talked to an advisor that had to negotiate the prenup for a client's son-in-law because he didn't want to have to have that conversation. Like, they're just doing much more for the same cost, right? So we look at that. I think AI, you have to look at it and say, "What can it do to enhance my client's experience? What can I do to add value but still make sure that I'm relevant?" So what are the things that are important to my client? Being able to pick up the phone and talk to me. Empathy when the market's down. There's a lot of different things. You know, being able to take the plan and take it to the next level. I was actually looking at an estate planning thing. I asked Claude, "Help think through this estate planning." And then from that, it still required other experts to do other things. So I would never have been able to have that kind of insight at my fingertips, but it still requires expertise. And so I look at it and say, if you look in history, we always tend to be able to take the technology, figure out how to do more, and that generates more jobs. We would never have imagined the types of jobs that have opened up. Think about all the people who own a business that now exists because the iPhone came along and cloud services came along, right? These were things we didn't even know we needed and suddenly we have access to them. And so I think you have to think of AI that way and not be afraid of it. But so then I shifted to another way and I've done it on Replit and various things. You have to do it. And here's—I'm going to give a couple examples that I think are really interesting. The Career Advice That Ruins Lives (And What Actually Works) - Bill Gurley

Anthony Scaramucci · 2026-03-17
Bill Gurley (General Partner, Benchmark) Anthony Scaramucci (Founder and Managing Partner, SkyBridge Capital)
> "The thing you never tried is the thing that eats at you as you get older.
TL;DR: True career security in the age of AI comes from pursuing deep "fascination" rather than traditional safety, as obsessive curiosity fuels the continuous learning and high energy required to reach the top 1% of any field. Regrets of Inaction: Citing Daniel Pink’s...

TL;DR: True career security in the age of AI comes from pursuing deep "fascination" rather than traditional safety, as obsessive curiosity fuels the continuous learning and high energy required to reach the top 1% of any field.

The Psychology of Career Regret

  • Regrets of Inaction: Citing Daniel Pink’s research, Gurley emphasizes that humans easily forgive themselves for "action mistakes" (failed ventures), but are haunted by "boldness regrets"—the things they never tried.
      • Statistical Dissatisfaction: Surveys by Wharton and SurveyMonkey indicate that 60% to 70% of college-educated professionals would start their careers over differently if given the chance.
  • The "Use It or Lose It" Proposition: Young people often view time as infinite, but by age 40 or 50, the weight of wasted time becomes heavy. Gurley argues that since most people will work 80,000 hours in their life, doing anything other than what they love is a massive strategic error.
  • Fascination vs. Passion: Gurley prefers the term "fascination" (borrowed from Jerry Seinfeld) over the cliché of "passion."
      • The Litmus Test: Fascination is defined by a natural desire to study the history, nuance, and greats of a field during your free time.

Mastery Through "Honing the Craft"

  • The Bob Dylan Model: Gurley describes Bob Dylan as a "historian" of folk music who studied the genre more intensely than anyone else before finding success.
      • Continuous Learning: Elite success is a "vector" of continuous learning. If studying your field feels like an alternative to watching Netflix, you are in the right lane.
  • The Artisan Approach: In an AI-dominated world, rote knowledge is no longer a competitive advantage.
      • Finding the Edge: Success now requires acting like a craftsman—studying the nuance and "edge" of a field where AI models lack data.
      • Mark Cuban’s AI Divide: Gurley cites Cuban’s observation that there are two types of people: those who use Large Language Models (LLMs) to learn faster, and those who use them to avoid learning.

Strategic Career Principles

  • Proactive Peer Groups: Gurley argues that the "lone wolf" mentality is a mistake. He advocates for creating a formal peer group of people on the same "rung of the ladder."
      • Shared Horsepower: A trusted peer group provides shared learning, access to combined networks, and emotional support during the inevitable "bad days."
  • Go to the Epicenter: For specific industries, geography remains a critical multiplier.
      • The Geographic Nexus: If you are serious about a field, move to its epicenter (e.g., Silicon Valley for tech, New York for finance). This "nexus" accelerates every other principle of success.
  • The "Don't Half-Ass It" Rule: Referencing Matthew McConaughey’s father, Gurley notes that choosing a dream career isn't about taking the easy way out; it is a "responsibility" to apply total effort.

Advice for Young Professionals

  • The Risk of "Safe" Jobs: Traditional "safe" paths are increasingly vulnerable to AI. The most "resourceful" version of a person only emerges when they are working on something they are passionate about.
  • Planning Over Paralysis: For those with financial constraints or debt, Gurley recommends using "scenario planning" and AI tools to build a five-step game plan before taking the leap.
  • Resourcefulness over Fear: Passion and fascination provide the "rocket fuel" necessary to overcome the fear of jumping off the cliff and "building the airplane on the way down."

***

Weekly Washington Policy Pulse: Iran, Basel III, Stablecoins, Warsh (March 16, 2026) | Balance...

Bloomberg Podcasts · 2026-03-17
President Trump is pushing for a "coalition of the willing" to secure the Strait of Hormuz amid escalating Iran tensions, while the financial sector prepares for the "Super Bowl" of regulation with the formal Basel III Endgame proposal this Thursday.
TL;DR President Trump is pushing for a "coalition of the willing" to secure the Strait of Hormuz amid escalating Iran tensions, while the financial sector prepares for the "Super Bowl" of regulation with the formal Basel III Endgame proposal this Thursday. Strait of Hormuz...

TL;DR

President Trump is pushing for a "coalition of the willing" to secure the Strait of Hormuz amid escalating Iran tensions, while the financial sector prepares for the "Super Bowl" of regulation with the formal Basel III Endgame proposal this Thursday.

Geopolitics and the Iran Conflict

  • Strait of Hormuz Escorts: President Trump has called for an international "coalition of the willing" (including China, the UK, Japan, South Korea, and Australia) to provide naval escorts for tankers.
      • International Response: Current feedback is muted; Japan, Australia, and Germany have indicated they have no immediate plans or have not received formal requests.
  • US-China Relations: Press Secretary Karoline Leavitt suggested President Trump may delay his planned summit with President Xi Jinping, stating the President will not leave U.S. soil "at times of war."
      • Trade Negotiations: U.S. and Chinese officials are currently in Paris negotiating tariffs, with outcomes expected shortly.
  • Defense Supplemental Funding: The Pentagon is reportedly seeking ~$50 billion in an Iranian supplemental defense bill.
      • Legislative "Christmas Tree": Congressional committees are already looking to attach riders, including $15 billion in farm aid and $20 billion for disaster insurance. The final package could reach $100–$200 billion, though floor action is unlikely before summer.

Banking and Financial Regulation

  • Basel III Endgame: The formal proposal is expected this Thursday, kicking off a 90-day comment period.
      • Capital Requirements: Requirements for operational and market risk are expected to increase, while credit risk for mortgages may decrease to incentivize bank originations.
  • G-SIB Surcharge: A "Method 2" tweak to the G-SIB surcharge is anticipated, which could provide approximately $32 billion in capital relief for the six largest "Global Systemically Important Banks" (JPMorgan, Bank of America, Wells Fargo, Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, and Citigroup).
  • Prediction Markets: The CFTC issued guidance classifying most prediction markets as "swaps" under the Commodities Exchange Act.
      • Rulemaking Timeline: A formal rule is not expected to be finalized until mid-2027, making it difficult for future administrations to quickly reverse the stance via simple guidance.

Digital Assets and Crypto Policy

  • Stablecoin Regulation: The Senate Banking Committee is expected to intensify work on a "clarity draft" regarding stablecoin yields in April.
  • OCC "Genius Act" Implementation: The Office of the Comptroller of the Currency (OCC) has proposed rules requiring stablecoin issuers to hold 100% high-quality liquid assets.
      • Yield Ban Controversy: The OCC proposal includes a provision that would prohibit "handshake agreements" between issuers and third-party platforms offering reward programs, a move heavily opposed by the crypto industry.
  • CBDC Ban: The Senate-passed housing bill includes a ban on a Central Bank Digital Currency (CBDC) through 2030, though House Republicans are pushing for a permanent ban.

Congressional and Administrative Outlook

  • Housing Legislation: The "21st Century for Road Housing Act" has moved to the House after passing the Senate.
      • Institutional Investors: A contentious provision would require single-family REITs to dispose of rental properties within seven years, which critics argue will stifle new construction.
  • The SAFE Act: President Trump has signaled he will not sign any legislation until this voter identification bill passes.
      • Filibuster Standoff: Senator John Thune (R-SD) lacks the 60 votes required for passage, but is expected to move through procedural motions to demonstrate effort to the White House.
  • Fed Chair Nomination: Senator Tom Tillis continues to block Kevin Warsh’s nomination for Federal Reserve Chair.
      • Interim Leadership: If the nomination remains stalled by May, Governor Miron may step in as acting Chair.

ICYMI: President Trump & JD Vance Take Questions About The Iran War In The Oval Office

Forbes Breaking News · 2026-03-17
Donald Trump (President of the United States) JD Vance (Vice President of the United States)
President Trump and Vice President JD Vance detailed the near-total decimation of Iran’s military and leadership following targeted strikes, while concurrently launching a massive domestic "fraud task force" to recoup billions in misappropriated funds from U.S.
TL;DR President Trump and Vice President JD Vance detailed the near-total decimation of Iran’s military and leadership following targeted strikes, while concurrently launching a massive domestic "fraud task force" to recoup billions in misappropriated funds from U.S. cities. ...

TL;DR

President Trump and Vice President JD Vance detailed the near-total decimation of Iran’s military and leadership following targeted strikes, while concurrently launching a massive domestic "fraud task force" to recoup billions in misappropriated funds from U.S. cities.

Military Strategy and the Iranian Conflict

  • Operational Success: President Trump claimed that U.S. military actions have effectively neutralized Iran’s offensive capabilities.
      • Asset Destruction: Iran has reportedly lost its entire navy, air force, and anti-aircraft weaponry, and is down to roughly 8% of its original missile and drone inventory.
      • B-2 Stealth Bombers: Trump praised the B-2’s role in the conflict, noting it is the only aircraft capable of carrying a 200,000-pound payload and was essential in destroying Iran’s "nuclear dust" and underground facilities.
  • Leadership Vacuum: Trump stated that two to three layers of Iranian leadership have been "extinguished," including the primary successors to the Supreme Leader.
  • Conflict Timeline: The President signaled that while he does not want a long war, the conflict will not end this week, though it will be "wrapped up soon" to ensure Iran never acquires a nuclear weapon.
  • Retaliatory Surprises: Trump admitted that experts were shocked when Iran launched thousands of missiles at neutral neighbors like the UAE, Saudi Arabia, Qatar, and Kuwait during the escalation.

Domestic Integrity and the "Fraud Czar"

  • Task Force Mandate: Vice President JD Vance embraced the title of "fraud czar," leading a task force designed to root out systemic corruption and "country-changing" amounts of stolen tax dollars.
  • Minnesota Medicaid Crackdown: Vance confirmed that the administration has halted Medicaid payments to Minnesota.
      • Enforcement: Dr. Mehmet Oz is working with local authorities to investigate pervasive fraud, specifically citing concerns regarding the Somali community and the flow of illicit cash through the Minneapolis airport.
  • Law Enforcement Integration: The task force will utilize the DOJ to pursue criminal prosecutions. Trump and Vance characterized much of the systemic fraud in New York, Chicago, and California as criminal rather than merely administrative.
  • Resource Allocation: Trump argued that recovering these funds would allow for substantial tax lowers for the American public and turn the national deficit into a "profitable country."

Allied Relations and Global Contributions

  • Burden Sharing: Trump expressed deep disappointment with NATO allies and Asian partners (Japan, South Korea, China) for their lack of military or financial support during the Iran operation.
      • Energy Protection: Trump noted that while Japan and China receive over 90% of their energy through the Strait of Hormuz, the U.S. is bearing the full cost and risk of securing the waterway.
  • Critique of the UK: Trump specifically criticized the United Kingdom’s Prime Minister for hesitating to send minesweepers and ships until after the U.S. had already secured the region.
  • Military Presence Abroad: The President questioned the continued deployment of 45,000–50,000 troops in Germany, Japan, and South Korea if those nations refuse to assist in U.S.-led security operations.

Legislative and Social Priorities

  • Save America Act: Trump is pushing for a Senate vote on a package that includes:
      • Voter Integrity: Mandatory photo ID, proof of citizenship for voting, and the elimination of mail-in ballots.
      • Cultural Issues: Federal bans on "transgender mutilation" (gender-affirming surgeries for minors) and men participating in women's sports.
  • Partisan Unity: Trump criticized Republican "independents" for failing to stick together like Democrats, whom he described as "glue" when voting on party-line issues.
  • DHS Funding: Trump blamed Democrats for a lack of funding for the Department of Homeland Security, claiming that 80% of the anti-terrorism task force is currently working without pay.

Future Foreign Policy: Cuba and China

  • China Excursion: Trump noted he has requested a delay for his planned trip to Beijing, stating it is imperative he remains in Washington until the Iranian conflict is further stabilized.
  • The "Taking" of Cuba: Trump described Cuba as a "failed nation" and suggested the U.S. is currently in a position to "free" or "take" the island in some form.
      • Economic Potential: He characterized the island as a high-value landscape for tourism and entrepreneurship once the "violent leadership" is addressed.
